dyspepsia and, of course, the first thing I did was to consult a physi-
cian. As I had always enjoyed the best of health I was worried a good
deal over this, my first illness, and my only desire was to get well as
speedily as I could. I took regularly all the medicines as my doctor
prescribed them. In a short time I felt all right, thought I was cured
and stopped taking the medicine. But it wasn't long before I was feel-
ing as badly as ever, and again I had recourse to the doctor. This
kept up for some time until at last I made up my mind I would quit
doctoring and try some of the remedies I saw advertised. 1 had read
so much of Ripans Tabules that I concluded I would give them
a good trial. It said ' one would give relief,' and I thought if one
could give relief a whole lot might cure me. I purchased a dollar's
worth, and when I had finished them I was feeling better than I had
for years. I continued to use them, and to-day I believe I am a per-
fectly well man. Once in a great while Ido have a slight touch of
the old malady, but a single Tabule fixes it all right i generally
keep a supply at home."

STORM CENTER OF MAGUIREiSM.
A CAREFUL analysis of Democratic events
and their causes thus far in the campaign in
dicate that the storm center of Maguireism in
this State is located somewhere in the vicinity of
Tulare. The Maguire idea originated in resolutions
passed by the County Committee of that county.
Thereupon Chairman Alton! of the State Committee,
who hails from Tulare, came north and appointed a
committee of nine to settle factional difficulties in this
city This committee surmounted all obstacles by ap
pointing a committee of one hundred and investing it
with full power to act.
Thus it will be perceived that Maguireism took its
root in Tulare, whence it has rapidly spread
throughout the State. Although Mr. Alford sought
to cover the tendrils with soil by passing through the
State Committee a declaration that the committees j
of nine and one hundred had been created only in the !
people's interest, they have since appeared upon the
tee. The latter committee has adopted a resolu- I
tion pledging its faith to Maguire, and a sub
committee has decided to appoint 154 delegates from
city in the interest of the Congressman's am-
The ITulare County tail may thus be said to be wag
.ging the Democratic California dog. Party inspira
tion having been obtained from that region, of course
it was eminently proper that political action should
start in the same quarter. But it may with consider
able propriety be asked, What interest has Tulare
County in the gubernatorial aspirations of Judge
Maguire? The county is agricultural in character.
Do the farmers of Tulare desire the adoption of the
single tax theory? It ought not to be necessary to
remind them that that theory contemplates placing all
the burdens of maintaining the Government on land.
Judge Maguire's idea is to destroy the fee-simple and
compel landowners to hold their estates subject to the
demands of the tax eaters.
â¢ I >r, perhaps the farmers of Tulare desire to pro
m< te the ancient feud of the Democracy with the
Southern Pacific Company. But if they do why are
they furthering Maguire's gubernatorial aspirations?
Huntington's funding scheme will not come before
the California Legislature. If their purpose is to
thwart the multifarious schemes of the railroad mag
nate, the farmers of Tulare. instead of putting up a
job to appoint a convention for the nomination of
Judge Magnire as Governor, should petition that gen
tleman to continue in Congress.
On the whole we see no reason why Tulare County
should be the storm center of Maguireism. Yet the
facts of the Democratic situation indicate that it is.
There are many saloons in this town at which a
copy of the Bulletin is given to each buyer of a drink.
Leaving aside the fact that this is a pretty mean trick
to play on a man who may be under the influence of
liquor, there is a natural curiosity to know whether
the twilight journalists count these gift enterprise
copies when they are making up a list of circulation
to which to swear.
Birdie Sanders of Idaho, a teacher, was attacked by
three men and, drawing a pistol, sent two to the
morgue and the other to the hospital. That young
woman ought to be able to teach the young idea how
. to shoot all right.
Since the man who shot Soapy Smith has followed
him. owing to wounds inflicted by the dying gambler,
it can only be hoped that Soapy has been so thor
oughly reformed that there will be no renewal of the
.trouble between them.
Confidence in General Sh.ilter is so well established
that when he calls the Governor of Santiago a pre
suming rascal there will be no doubt but that is ex
actly what the Governor is.
Possibly young Leiter had $5,000,000 worth of fun
out of his little wheat experiment, but his father had
the bill to pay. and it is not easy to see where the old
man's fun came in.
Cubans are pleading for self-government, but the
best way for them to get it is to prove that there is
something to them besides appetite.
According to Sagasta Spaniards "die but never
surrender." For the first word read "lie" and a flood
of light is thrown on the situation.
Rumors are heard that Cervera intends to go to
Boston to live. Perhaps he feels boun>- to do pen
ance for having lost his fleet.
Captain Sampson of the navy has met no Delilah
" with shears, but a number of people seem to be after
him with knives.
â  .â¢ Correspondents who are trying to stir up trouble in
Cuba ought to be given space in a cemetery at regular
rates.
With California boys at the front, this State regards
Manila as good as a fallen city right now.
GOULD (\S (\ KfILSOMINE ARTIST.
AS a whitewash expert there is probably none
rising superior to Frank H. Gould. He
realizes the necessity of a coating to hide the
real character of himself and his associates, and with
lavish hands he daubs it on. To knock off a few
flakes of the deceptive exterior becomes a duty, and
if some skin peel with it the fault is Gould's.
The Agnews investigation has gone far enough to
demonstrate the purpose dishonest, the method far
cical, and the result, all arranged in advance, to be
the inevitable glossing over of faults so grave that
they have made the asylum reek with scandal. At
taches are afraid to speak. Outsiders are not per
mitted to tell what they know. The fear of Gould has
closed the mouths of subordinates, and the creatures
of Gould aid him in keeping silent those who would
be glad to speak. The way in which the investiga
tion is being hampered is in itself a plea of guilty.
Were the institution run honestly and properly, the
personnel respectable, everybody concerned would be
eager to have the fullest light turned upon its internal
affairs. Instead of this, those against whom charges
have been brought assume the role of witnesses,
judge, jury and prosecutor. It could hardly be ex-
pected under these circumstances that the truth would
be encouraged. Gould sloshes into the whitewash
bucket, and, as far as his brush can reach, plasters
his environment with the bogus emblem of purity.
Doubtless Sponogle. a man of ill repute, and the
weakling associates of Gould will emerge from the
ordeal snowy as alabaster images, walking monu
ments of virtue.
Gould has a faculty for holding office. He is the
gentleman who recently made report to Governor
Budd on the condition of building and loan associa
tions, and afterward made a second report for publi
cation, conflicting with the first, explaining that the
first came from him as an official, and the second as
an individual. Here is a delicate distinction. Either
the official or the individual did something which
either the official or the individual had no right to do.
Just where the two entities merge is known only to
Gould, and while he is in the whitewash line he would
better give each of them a dab.
It was Gould who while Speaker of the Assembly
in 1893 presented a bill for services at $18 per day,
which an honest Controller did not let him get. A
member of the Assembly is allowed $8 a day and the
Speaker $10. According to Gould's figures he was
entitled to both salaries, and he had the nerve to
press the claim even after it had once been thrown
out. His course is now easy to understand. That
was only another instance of his dual existence. If he
can be an officer and an individual, neither entity
having anything in common with the other, surely
he could be an Assemblyman and a Speaker, two dis
tinct personalities, but with one pocket. However,
the record does not look well, and a coating of white
wash is needed there, too, if any be left when the
Agnews comedy is over. Imagine such other Speakers
as Frank L. Coombs or Morris M. Estee trying so
knavish a trick.
In the news columns the shameful tale of Agnews
has been told. The conditions prevailing there are
a reproach to the State. There is no disposition oji
the part of Gould to clean out the place; it would
be hara-kiri. A cleaning would oust his brother and
other friends, bounce Sponogle and Hale and leave
Gould himself standing pensively outside the walls
and marveling what had struck him. Governor
Budd has devoted much time to State institutions,
particularly those having to do with the care of the
poor and afflicted. He owes it to himself now to
devote special attention to Agnews. If he desires to
go from office with honor he will have to see that
Gould and the Gould gang go first.
SfIMPSON'S REPORT.
SAMPSON'S report of the destruction of the
Spanish fleet off Santiago was well worth all
the space it occupied in the news of the day,
and all the time given by the public in reading it.
While it added nothing to what was already known of
the conflict, it possessed the peculiar eloquence that
ever lies in the words of those who "speak with
authority." It was official, and in the sedate language
of a formal document confirmed every essential detail
given by more picturesque and glowing writers of
the great victory.
Some regret will be felt that the rear admiral did
not in his report give special mention and honor to
Commodore Schley, who was in actual command of
the squadron during the battle. This would have been
the more graceful on his part because in reporting to
the admiral the commodore had said: "I congratulate
you most sincerely upon this great victory of the
squadron under your command." The opportunity
thus afforded for an interchange of congratulations
was so good that a mistake was made when the ad
miral allowed it to pass without profiting by it.
Californians will note with proud satisfaction that
our home-built battleship, the Oregon, receives from
the admiral higher praise than that accorded to any
other ship in the fleet. In narrating the events of the
fight as they occurred, the report says: "The Brook
lyn and the Texas had at the start the advantage of
position, and the Brooklyn maintained this lead until
the Oregon, steaming with amazing speed from the
commencement of the action, took first place."
Later on, in summing up the performances of the
various vessels and awarding due praise to each, the
admiral says: "The fine speed of the Oregon enabled
her to take a front position in the chase, and the
Cristobal Colon did not give up until the Oregon had
thrown a thirteen-inch shell beyond her. This per
formance adds to the already brilliant record of this
fine battleship and speaks highly of the skill and care
with which her admirable efficiency has been main
tained during a service unprecedented in the history
of vessels of her class."
There was nothing invidious in this praise given to
the ship that so fitly represents the ship-building
work of the Pacific Coast. That it was merited and
deserved has been attested by all who watched the
fight intelligently. In fact every report that has been
made of the battle has given special honor to the
Oregon, and the official report of the admiral has
done no more than confirm the glorious records of
her performances in the action that have been al
ready published.
The admiral's report was' accompanied by the re
ports made to him by the various commanding offi
cers of the fleet. All of these are documents which
our people can read with a justly founded pride. It
is notable that the commanders without exception
speak in highest praise of their seamen and give to
these unnamed heroes full credit for their share of the
great victory. Captain E%'ans (Fighting Bob) sums
up their conduct in a characteristic phrase: "So long
as the enemy showed his flag they (the crew) fought
like American seamen, but when the flag came down
they were as gentle and as tender as American
women." Courage in action and generosity in vic
tory could go no further, nor the highest chivalry be
better exemplified.
Why did the Post announce that the ex-Queen h
a victim of cancer? There are so many truths to tell
that fiction seems superfluous.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL,, THURSDAY, JULY 28, 1898.
THE NEED OF FOREST PROTECTION.
THE Brooklyn Eagle refers in somewhat severe
terms to the destruction of Western forests, even
in the Federal reservations, as a result of with
drawing troops to serve in a foreign war. We are
glad that the interest of the whole country in the
forests of a part of it is receiving attention, even
though the treatment of it may be partial and incom
plete.
The forests of a country are perhaps its most im
portant and valuable physical feature. They bear a
relation to the precipitation and distribution of that
moisture upon which the fertility of the soil de
pends, for which, in the regions which they dominate,
there is no possible substitute. Their destruction is an
artificial interference with the ways of nature, which
is sure of punishment, in a dehydrated soil and a loss
of fertility.
This statement of the relation of forests to fertility
has been contradicted by some superficial observers,
who cite the prairie States of the Upper Mississippi
Valley as an example of the non-concurrence of for
ests with moisture and fertility.
The contradiction is only apparent and is not real.
Those prairie regions differ in their physical features
from the region of high plateaus, mountains and val
leys west of the hundredth meridian.
In the prairie country nature provided a substitute
for the forests as an agent in the collection and dis
tribution of moisture. The prairie States have but
few altitudes greater than 600 feet. In their primitive
\ condition they were threaded by sloughs, drainage
j channels with but little fall, where the shed waters
j moved but slowly, and evaporation during most of the
year was not rapid. Here accumulated a vegetable
mold many feet in depth from the roots and the de
cay of the hummock grasses, which held water like
a sponge. These sloughs were reinforced by swamps,
marshes, ponds and small lake?, all serving as catch
ment basins for the rainfall, and most of them de
i fended against evaporation by the overgrowing
; grasses.
The moisture for the whole region evaporates from
the surface of the great lakes, from Superior to Erie.
Falling upon the prairie watersheds, this moisture
found its way to the natural catchment basins and
! supplied local evaporation, in the hot summer, for the
I seasonable thunderstorms by which it was distributed
to moisten the crust of the earth, refresh growing
crops and find its way to storage in the clay subsoil
to be drawn up by capillary attraction, and to give life
to the deep roots of trees. Within a quarter of a cen
tury grew up a rage for draining these sloughs,
swamps, marshes, ponds and lakes. Congress un
wisely passed "swamp land" laws, which permitted
the cheap alienation to private ownership of these
; natural catchment basins. They are now plowed
', fields, underlaid with drain tile. The spongy character
â  of the soil, so well calculated for retaining water, has
i been destroyed by cultivation. The usual percentage
of moisture, derived directly from the great lakes,
! falls every year, but finch no resting-place. It is
drained quickly through the former catchment basins,
goes speedily into the watercourses which have be
] come torrential, and passes down them destructively,
: deepening their narrowing channels, and is lost to
i its former useful purpose of supplying the summer
j showers and being by them distributed for moistening
! the crust of the earth. The melancholy results are
| beginning to appear. Along the watercourses, and for
! a considerable distance on each side, there were for
merly fine bodies of timber. On the river benches, or
intervales, this timber consisted of willow, cotton
wood, linn, sycamore and soft maple. Next there
were fine growths of white and black walnut,
white and black ash, hackberry and hickory.
Then on the clay hills were found white, black, red,
burr and pin oak, shagbark hickory and hard maple,
among which lumbermen recognized the most valu
able hardwoods on the continent.
These riparian forests have been too indiscrim
inately cut away and wantonly destroyed. But what
fire and ax spared is being rapidly swept away by
drainage of the sloughs and natural storage reser
voirs. The failure in distribution of moisture from
them has dried the crust of the earth so that all trees
of the hickory family, the genus Carya, are dying.
They are tap root trees, and, the earth having dried
out below their roots, they get no moisture and dis
appear. So far the trees which spread their roots
near the surface manage to live on the small quantity
of moisture supplied by direct precipitation, but their
doom will come soon or late.
In that prairie region, then, we see that drainage
j of natural reservoirs is in all respects equivalent to
I the deforestation of a timbered region, and that it car
! ries with it a gradual destruction of the tree-producing
capacity of the soil, which naturally bore timber, just
as in the timbered region successive fires destroy that
capacity.
This whole subject of nature's way of lifting mois
ture by evaporation from the bosom of the lakes and
seas, carrying it inland on the winds in certain sea
sons when their direction was favorable and there
storing it, either in soil sheltered by forests or in
; sloughs, swamps and marshes, either to be distributed
! by percolation into streams from which it might be
artificially diverted for irrigation, or by the same
means lifted up for distribution by the varying winds
of summer when the great currents from their orig
inal source have ceased, is of fascinating interest to
] the student of physical economics. It requires to be
! studied and that the conclusions reached be practically
: applied, or our fertile regions will one day be as blear
as the sands that drift over the ruins of Persepolis,
where â

fad for making all naval officers almost too pretty to
do anything but sit before a camera in their best
clothes.

wrestling with a dose of measles, to learn that several
gentlemen at our own Camp Merritt have had a sim
ilar experience.

that the Oregon is the best battle-ship in the navy.
It was built with the idea of being something of the

VALMORE'S WONDERFUL VOICE.
THERE is In this city at present a
young man who is billed In large
letters "The Human Brass Band."
The gift he has is unique, its cul-
tivation wholly self-taught. There
are others able to Imitate one or two in
struments, but this young man imitates
them all. With equal facility he is the
bassoon, making the air tremulous with
the depth of notes which seems below any
possible range of the voice, and the next
instant he gives the quivering melody of
the violin, rising higher and higher, true
as if drawn by a bow in the hands of a
master.
Valmore is the most unassuming of
mortals. He is rather under the average
size, a decided blonde, with a suggestion
of strawberry tint. In conversation hl3
voice is light and delicate. There is noth
ing about its apparent quality to indi
cate its wondrous range and power. Val
more cannot explain the source of his
strange faculty. He simply knows that
he has it, and is making the most of it.
He is one of a family of sixteen, and
neither his brothers nor sister share with
him in any measure the tendency to be
a brass band. While yet a boy he real
ized that he could reproduce sounds, and
in his school days often did so for the
amusement of his playmates, who would
erect a tent, charging a pin for admission,
and under the tent Valmore would be the
entire show. As he grew older the de
sire to imitate various instruments as well
Sawing Wood Comes Easy.
as vocal peculiarities grew stronger, and
he practiced it, with no thought of going
on the stage until five years ago. At
that time he became a professional, and
his success has bees mark.-. 1. He is now
under engagement for a three years' tour
abroad.
As interesting as Valinore Is on the
stage he Is seen and heard to best ad
vantage among his friends. To them he
gives some exhibitions in which he does
not yet regard himself as sufficiently per
fected to try on the public. As In rapid
succession he accomplishes feats of vo
cal gymnastics which excite marvel, he
explains how' he has tried and. retried
certain acts or how the idea came to him.
He has that absolute lack of self-con
sciousness which is the truest modesty,
and achievements which cause astonish
ment to the witnesses appear to him to
be a matter of course. "Oh, I'll do better
than that," he says, "after a little more
practice."
On a recent overling after the regular
performance Valmore met a few ac
quaintances, and that he afforded them a
delightful hour any of them will attest.
He did not confine, himself to musical
subjects, for the first imitation was that
of the phonograph. There was the pre-
He Mimics the Phonograpf).
llminary "buzz-z" and the whirr, followed
by the squeaky voice which is only
heard in this mechanism, and then the
sudden click announcing the end of the
message. One listening with closed eyes
would have sworn to having listened to

VISITING GERMANYâ lnquirer, Sau
salito, Cal. If a native of Germany leaves
his country after having performed mili
tary service or has received a certificate
permitting him to emigrate, and subse
quently becomes a citizen of the United
States, he can return to his native coun
try at any time after having acquired
ciilzenship without fear of molestation
from the Germany Government. If he
left his country witnout having per
formed military service, and becomes a
citizen of the United States, he may, in
time of peace, return and visit hia native
country for a period not exceeding four
months, by courtesy of the German Gov
ernment, but if he overstays that time he
is liable to arrest and impessment in the
army.

equal ease he can pursue a tenor beyond
the spires. Nothing in his natural voice
would indicate the least talent in the for
mer direction, a fact which renders the
truth all the more amazing.

tired. My vocal chords are used to it, for
I practice every day, and I enjoy it. I
make my living from it. and It Is a pleas
ure to me at the same time. '

FIGHTING 808 EVANSâ S. A. M.,
City. Robley D. Evans, now of the Iowa!
generally known as "Fighting Bob Bv
ans," was born in Virginia. He was
placed in command of the Torktown
and sent to Valparaiso. He anchored in
front of the water batteries defending
the city. The Chileans had a number of
torpeao boats with long arms, on which
torpedoes were placed. The boats were
being maneuvered in an unpleasant and
threatening manner for some time. Ev
ans watched those movements until they
became unbearable. He had the guns of
the lorktown loaded and the ship cleared
for action, after which he got into his
cutter and went to the commanding gen
eral, told him he wanted the torpedo boat
business stopped and gave him half an
hour to take them out of the harbor.
Whether right or wrong, his request was
complied with. It was there, from the
stand he took, that he gained the name
of "Fighting Bob." He it was who told
Secretary Herbert that it would please
him to make Spanish the prevailing lan
guage in hell for the next five years.

DEAD WOMAN'S WATCH.
Police Vainly Trying to Locate the
Important Clew.
The police are still seeking for the
watch supposed to have been taken from
the room of Sadie Carpenter the night
she was murdered. One of Chief Lees'
sleuths Is also keeping a close watch of
Joe Lochner, and he will not be allowed
to leave the city for a while. Although
nothing h;ia been found against the Ger
man, the nolice still believe he is the
murderer.
Lochner is a homeless outcast. All his
former friends and acquaintances have
cut him, and he is not allowed to frequent
his old haunts. When he was released
from prison ho went to the Hubbard
House, but was refused accommodations.
Proprietor Raulet was notitled by his
tenants that they would all leave if Loch
ner was permitted to lodge in the build-
Ing. He took his belongings and departed,
and as he is without money or friends it
is not known where he sleeps.
Mrs. Anderson, Sadie's mother, return
ed to Monterey yesterday morning. She
will apply for letters of administration in
Monterey bounty on the estate of the de
ceased. Nothing has been learned re
garding the whereabouts of H. S. Carpen
ter.

Mrs. Catherine J. Whelan secured a di

was allowed to resume her maiden name.
Asmussen, and was granted the custody
Qt her minor child.

TROUBLE ON THE ARIZONA
SAILORS' UNION OBJECTS TO
HER CHINESE CREW.
Mariposa Brings News of Annexation
of the Santa Cruz and Duff
Groups of Islands by
England.
The Philadelphia got away for Hono
lulu at 3 p. m. yesterday. Early in the
morning Rear-Admiral Miller transferred
his flag from the Albatross to the war
ship and a salute of thirteen guns was
iired. From that on everything went â 
with a rush on the Philadelphia, and at
2:30 p. m. she was ready for a start. A
last launch was sent ashore to gather up
the last of the 4nail and any stragglers.
Half an hour later everything was ready
for a start. The anchor was up, a full
head of steam was on the boilers, and at
3:05 p. m. the cruiser was on her way to
the flag-raising which is to take place at
Honolulu. Passing the naval reserve I
steamer Marion the admiral was saluted \
with thirteen guns, and the gunners on
the Philadelphia replied. Alcatraz and
Black Point followed suit and then Fort
Point took it up, so the last thing the I
men on the cruiser heard as they passed
out through the Golden Gate was the
boom of cannon.
As soon as the American flag has been
hoisted over the Hawaiian Islands the
men on the Philadelphia are in hopes that
their vessel will be suit on to Manila. The
repairs to the gunboat Yorktown are now
almost completed, and it is thought that
as soon as she is ready she will be sent to
Honolulu to relieve the Philadelphia
and the latter will be sent to join L>ew
ey's squadron at Manila.
The trouble over the Chinese crew of
the transport Arizona is not yet settled
by any manner of means. It appears that '
their retention by the United States Gov- j
eminent was part of the agreement when
the vessel was sold. At any rate they are
retained on the vessel and will go out on.
her when she sails for Manila by orders ;
from the powers that be at Washington. I
Yesterday Andrew Furuseth, secretary of
the Coast Seamen's Union, called upon I
Captain Robinson, U. S. A., in regard to 'â 
the matter. The captain conducted the j
negotiations for the purchase of the ves
eel on behalf of the United States, and \
Mr. Furuseth went to him because he had
control of the matter. A very heated ar- j
gument followed, and the Seamen's I
Union's secretary said, among other j
things, that it was un-Amerlean to em
ploy Chinese cheap labor when there
were plenty of good American sailors to i
be had for the asking. At this Captain j
Robinson got angry, and he is reported to
have said that he wasn't going to be bull- i
dozed by any labor union in the country, j
That he was as good an American as any 1
man in the United States and that he !
would run his own business to suit him- I
self and without outside interference.
"The Chinese crew of the Arizona was
shipped by me under orders from Wash
ington and they will remain on the ves
sei until I get orders from the same
source to discharge them," said he, as he
fro~.vm-d Mr. Furuseth out.
The seamen have not given the matter
up by any means, and a committee has
been appointed to interview Congressman
Maguire, and through him have a strong
representation of the case sent to Wash
ington. Mr. Furuseth says that no stone
will be left unturned to prevent a vessel
owned by the United States, and In use
as a transport, being manned by Chinese
labor.
Captain Walter S. Milnor, who brought '
the Arizona from Puget Sound to this i
port, left last night for Seattle and Cap- j
tain Robinson leaves to-day also for the !
same place. The latter will examine the
new transports Athenian and Tartar, and
Captain Milnor will bring one of them to
San Francisco.
The following notice to mariners has
been issued by the Lighthouse Inspector:
"The steam whistlo on Ban Francisco j
light vessel 70 wil not be in operation to- !
day: in case of fog the bell will be
sounded.
"The, steam whistle will again be In
operation, if possible, on the 20th."
The trouble lies with the engineer's de
partment. Yesterday Captain Lowell was
Informed that there was no more coal on
the lightship, so he had to secure a pass
age on one of the pilot boats and come
ashore in a hurry. A supply of black
diamonds will be put on boardto-day, and !
to-night the siren will be again in work- j
Ing order.
The Oceanic Steamship Company's
Mariposa arrived from Sydney, via Auck
land. Apia and Honolulu, yesterday. On
the 25th inst. the Rio de Janeiro was met.
Captain Ward reported all well
aboard the transport, and put aboard the
Mariposa a package of mail for San
Francisco. The Mariposa brought up
$1,250,000 in treasure and a large number
of passengers. One of the latter was E.
Klnath. frÂ«.m Apia. He was a member of
the Sophie Sutherland expedition, and
nearly died from malarial foyer in the
Solomon group. He says that Soronson
led them all a wild goose chase, and the
only trace of gold they found was in the
interior of an island, where it was im
possible to get at. The Sutherland is now
OUt sixteen days from Apia, and the only
ones left aboard of all who sailed from
this port on her are Captain Alec Mc-
Lean and a man named Higgins.
The Mariposa brings the nows that the
I British cruiser Mohawk had raised the
j English flag over the Santa Cruz and
Duff groups of islands. These were the
only unannexed Island? In the southern
seas, and thinking some one else might
claim them England seized the lot. They
lie between the Solomons and New
Hebrides, and are of more strategical
value than anything else. The Santa Cruz
group Is oompopr-d <>f seven large islands
and the Duff or Wilson group of eleven.
: The natives are cannibals, and will al
ways be remembered for the murder of
; Bishop Patteson and Commodore Good
enough.
The sister ships Alcinous and Alcidos
arrived in port yesterday. The Alcinous
took 159 days to come from Ardrossan,
Scotland, and the Alcidps 1,"4 days to
come from Antwerp. Both masters re
port an unusual number of calms and
i fight winds.

CONDUCTING A BANKING GAME
The Cases of W. D. Valentine and
M. B. Burke Dismissed.
The case of W. D. Valentine and M.
R. Burke, charged with conducting a
banking game at 19 Ellis street, was
concluded before Judge Conlan yester
day, and the Judge dismissed both cases.
A similar case was brought against
Valentine recently, where he was
charged with conducting a percentage
game, and Judge Cook, on appeal, ac
quitted him. Judge Conlan, in view of
that decision and also In view of the fact
that in his opinion the evidence for the
prosecution did not show that a banking
game was carried on within the meaning
of the statute, dismissed the cases.
Frankie Payne, who operated the wires
in a room in the Parrott building, con
necting with 19 Ellis street, was subpe
naed to appear as a witness for the prose
cution, but did not make her appearance.

Mrs. Vlctorina Fershaw, the Danish
â woman who arrived on the steamer Star
buck and was refused a landing because
ehe was a pauper and an Invalid, has at
last been allowed to go ashore. A bond
for $500 was given- that she will not be
come a public charge. Mrs. Willard B.
Harrington has taken her In charge and
had her removed to the Children's Hospi
tal for treatment.
HE DOES NOT
WANT A SUIT
J. J. Valentine Fears the
Stamp Act.
AN APPEAL TO MERCHANTS \
PRESIDENT OF WELLS-FARGO
TALKS QUITE FREELY.
A Strong Plea That No Action Be
Taken Here Until the Test
Cases in the East Are
Decided.
That J. J. Valentine, president of Wells,
Fargo & Co., Is very desirous of having
the local merchants drop the proposed
suit for violating the law in collecting
from Its patrons the 1-cent tax that the
corporation should pay fs apparent from
his actions during the past week. When
the local merchants appealed to the Mer
chants' Association for support in their
protest against the injustice and the lat
ter body decided to fight the corporation
in the Federal courts, Mr. Valentine made
haste to call upon the officers of the as
sociation and request that they postpone
taking such steps until similar cases
should be decided in the courts of the
East. Several interviews were held by
him with the officers of the body, and
yesterday he was given permission to ad
dress the board of directors at a special
meeting.
Mr. Valentine stated that at least a
dozen suits were now pending against the
express companies in different parts of
the United States, and, therefore, he be
lieved no benefit would possibly be gained
by having an additional suit brought in
this city. He had received dispatches j
from Chicago that the suit pending
against the express company there had i
already been submitted to the court, and
that a decision was daily expected. He
assured the board that his company
would promptly abide by the decision of
the courts and would place no obstacles i
in the way of a speedy decision.
The board decided to telegraph at once
to the Merchants' Association in New
York and Chicago to ascertain whether
the pending suits against the express
companies were being duly prosecuted,
and how soon a decision would be reached.
If the answers to these telegrams show
that the actions are being properly prose
cuted and that a decision will be reached
within thirty days, the board decided to
postpone the suit contemplated in this
city. If, however, no early decision will
be reached in the Eastern suits, the board
decided to proceed with the action here.
The necessary evidence has been secured
and will be held ready for any possible
future use.
In speaking of the position of Mr.
Valentine as president of the express
company, one of the directors said yes
terday that the corporation's officer's de
sire that no action be taken seemed to
be based more upon personal pride than
anything else. In the meeting he called
attention to the fact that the company
has always been on the best terms with
the merchants of this city, and he would
not like to have it go afcroad that there
Is any cause for complaint at this day.
If the present troubles can be bridged
over by the decisions that will be ren
dered in the test cases in the East it
will look better for the local branch of
I the company than it would to have an
other suit on its hands.
Prior to the meeting Mr. Valentine ad
dressed a long letter to Acting President
Baldwin, inclosing a clipping from a rail
road paperâ the Railway Ageâ which, of
course, takes the position that the tax
upon common carriers should be paid by
the people, and not by the corporations.
In Concluding the letter he says:
We reppect the riffht of private Judgment,
prize the good opinion of the public and de
sire the pood will of the community which,
despite the contention now perhaps disturbing
its ordinary even fl>w. we claim to deserve and
hope ever to enjoy. With utmost respect, I am,
very truly yours.
He Has No Redress.
VS. TI. Wright, a "stlllman" in the em
ploy of the Pacific Oil Company at Ala
meda, was Injured by the breaking of
the bottom of one of the vats in which
oil is refined, and he brought an action
against the company for damages, alleg
ing negligence on the part of his em
ployers In not having safe and suitable
vessels and appurtenances at their works.
In the Superior Court of Alameda County
he received judgment for damages. The
case v.as appealed to the Supreme Court,
which yesterday reversed the judgment
of the lower court, holding that a part of
ihc duties of the stlllman was to see that
things in his department were In order,
and that if he was hurt he was guilty of
contributory negilgence.
Precinct Registration Officers.
The Board of BtoCtlOS Commissioners
met yesterday and resumed the work of
selecting precinct registration officers.
When the board adjourned last evening
selections had been made in all but three
Assembly districts. The work will be re
sumed this morning, but It will not be
completed in time to have a. report ready
to present when the commission meets at
11 o'clock to transact the usual routine
business. Commissioners Broderick and
Creswfcll stated last evening that it was
likely that the names of the precinct
registration officers selected would not be
made known until Friday.

that the transport Indiana was

nila. The monitor Monadnock sailed
from Honolulu for the Philippines

displayed on her run from this port to

Manila by August 8.

which have prevented the transfer of

structions for the Seventh California

AN UNEXPLODED SHELL.
A Souvenir From a Spanish
Cruiser Captured by Ad
miral Dewey.
G. W. Samuel, of 330% Fourth street,
has in his possession a relic of the pres
ent war, which, to use his own words,
"money can not buy." It is nothing more
or less than a two and a half-inch shell,
that was taken from tho Spanish auxil
iary cruiser, Callao, when that vessel waa
captured in Manila Bay by Admiral Dew
ey. The shell has boen weighed by Mr.
Samuel, and its weight is over eight
pounds. The base or cap of the shell
is of a brass and copper alloy, and It is
in this that the powder for its discharge
is loaded. The head of the shell Is fil!-d
with smaller iron balls that scatter when
the shell explodes. The explosion is by
concussion. This kind of missile, which is
one of the most deadly known, is very
effective at short range. This most In
teresting relic of the war was made a
present to Mr. Samuel by Mr. Bafandio,
the freight clerk of the steamer Aztec,
which has just returned from Hongkong.
The shell was brought over from Manila

South Dakota Battalion
and Recruits.
The Third Battalion South Dakota Vol
unteers, the Thirteenth Minnesota re
cruits and 155 of the First Colorado re
cruits will break camp at 8 o'clock this
morning, and as soon as they can get
their tents rolled up will take up their
march for the transport St. Paul.
The line of march will be much the
same as that of others; down Point Lobos
avenue to Central, along Central to Post,
down Post to Van Ness, thence to Golden
Gate avenue, and down Market to East
street and to the transport.
The Colorado recruits that will be left
behind were yesterday placed under the
command of Lieutenant Colonel Barnett
of the Tenth Pennsylvania, as were the
four companies of recruits from Oregon,

EIGHTH CALIFORNIA.
The Medical Supplies Are Re-
ceived and Vaccination Is to
Be Begun This Morning.
CAMP BARRETT. Bather Station, July
27.â Major W. F. Dudley to-day received
a complete line of medical supplies from
the United States army depot in San j
Francisco. It includes every article :
necessary for the practice of surgery and j
medicine, all put up in uniform-sized j
wooden chests. In fact, it Is a complete
drugstore, with the addition of a modern
library. There is a medical case, a dress- I
Ing or surgeon's case and a field desk.
The desk is equipped with all the neces
sary blanks and forms, besides pen, ink,
pencils, etc. In speaking of this outfit.
Major Dudley said: "I have had consid
erable experience in hospital work, but I
have never seen a more complete equip
ment than this. It has everything from
penpoints to a library for the use of the
hospital corps, and will place us in a posi
tion to meet any kind of case presented."
Vaccine points were received to-day,
and the work of vaccinating the 1300 men
of the regiment will be begun to-morrow
(Thursday) morning. It is not expected
that more than one company will be
taken a day, but there will be no delay
in putting all through the operation. The
ladies' social at one of the prominent
theaters In a short time.

ORDERS TO MOVE RESCINDED.
The Seventh California Will Not Be Transferred
From Camp Merritt to the Presidio.
Contrary to the orders of Tuesday the Seventh Regiment of California
Volunteers will not move to the Presidio this morning. Colonel Berry
commanding the regiment called upon General Merriam yesterday after
noon and during a long conversation stated that his men had already
had two weeks' experience at the Presidio and that they were content to
remain where they were until they are embarked for the Philippines.
Though the camp equipment was nearly all packed and ready for the
vans, General Merriam immediately sent to General Miller at Camp Mer
ritt telegraphic instructions to rescind the orders Issued on Tuesday.
General Miller at once communicated these instructions to the officers
location,
of the Seventh and until It sails the regiment will remain In its present
It Is expected that the Fifty-first lowa will move for the Presidio to
morrow or the next day. The desire of the regiment is to get away from
Camp Merritt just as soon as orders can be Issued.

a difficult problem to handle.

A CLASH IN HIGH RANK.
Co 1 .. Barber of New York and
Col Stover of South Dakota
Spar for Command.
Lieutenant Colonel Lee Stover, com
manding the troops for the transport St.
Paul, and Colonel Thomas H. Barber,
commanding the New York volunteers
and a great deal of Influence, had some
little difference yesterday over the juris
diction of matters on the transport. It
has since been amicably arranged.
Although Colonel Stover has been or
dered by the department to take com
mand of all the troops on shipboard, he
yesterday found upon his arrival at the
dock that Colonel Barber had in a great
measure relieved him of his duties, like
wise his stateroom. As Colonel Barber
is accompanying the expedition on de
tached service and had no orders *o
usurp the office delegated to Colonel Sto
ver his right to the stateroom and to the
command were vigorously disputed In
fact, the lieutenant colonel went so far
as to lock his stateroom against the fur
ther ingress of Colonel Barber, and for a
time things looked interesting.
The matter was finally disposed of by
referring it to the department. As a re
sult Colonel Barber will command the
St. Paul until the boat's arrival at Hono
lulu, and after that Colonel Stover will
have his chance.

THE ANGLER AND THE PIKE.
How Two Noted Fishermen Were
Trapped and the Wrong Man
Accused.
F. M. Haight, a popular salesman of
Clabfough, Golcher & Co/ was agreeably
surprised on Tuesday afternoon when a
messenger from Wells, Fargo & Co. hand
ed him a package which contained a
large fish.
There were quite a few prominent ang
lers in the store at the time of the ar
rival of the supposed lordly representa
tive of Russian River and wagers were
qtiickly made as to what variety of the
fresh water denizen the package con
tained.
Some guessed black bass, others striped
bass, others steelhead, but Will Golcher
having caught a glimpse of the six-pound
monster through the sack, ventured to
say pike or "hard mouth," more com
monly called.
Golcher hit the nail on the head. When
the sack in which the llsh was neatly
bound was opened, Mr. Pike put in a
very strong appearance, much to the dis
gust of the consignee and the pleasure
of the witnesses who subsequently drank
to the health and success of Mr." Haight
and the friend who was so thoughtful as
to ship him a "prize beauty" from the
far distant fishing ground.
Haight was nonplused. He thought of
all manner of schemes regarding the
proper disposal of the pike, and finally
struck an idea.
"There is 'Honest' John Butler, who al
ways remembers me whenever he goes
bass fishing." thought Haight to himself
and here is an opportunity of returning
compliments," etc.
Quickly the name Haight was erased-
T ro u m t J5 e Wells-Fargo tag and the name
John Butler substituted. The fish was
again shipped through the express com
pajiy to Mr. Butler, who received it right
side up with care.
It is hardly necessary to state that But
ler was a target for many hard jokes
and expressions of fishy thoughts from
friends who witnessed the unpacking- of
the poor old pike.
t "* Now - Ti ha l d 0 you tnink of m >' frlend
John Gallagher, who wag kind enough
to ship me this beautiful fish free of
charge?" remarked Butler, as his eagle
eyes feasted on the unpalatable king of
the pike family. "I know what I shall
do with it. I will present it to some of
my cash customers who cannot tell the
difference between a pike and a king
salmon."
Butler then addressed a letter to Mr
Gallagher, who is angling for bass on
Russian River, thanking him for kind re
tn< mbrance and intimating that a bass
wruld be preferable to a pike. When
Gallagher received Butler'B letter he an
swered by telegraph:
"What's the matter with you, John?
Got 'em again? I haven't shipped you a
fish of any kind. GALLAGHER."

owner of the sloop Edna, which defeated

Sager will lill the trophy, which is a ca
pacious and handsome punchbowl, with
the products of the Eisen Vineyard, and
fairly shook the roofs of the great train
shed. We always knew that California
was 'all right,' but we were doubly sure
of it when v.'c had crossed its borders.
Four hours later we were at Oakland,
where we waited until 7 o'clock Thursday
morning, and then boarded the great
steamer Piedmont for San Francisco.
"At the San Francisco pier we were met
by a fine band, the First Tennessee,
which escorted us to a large hall, beau
tifully decorated with flowers and flags.
Here the Red Cross Society gave us a
royal welcome and a much appreciated
lunch. It is no exaggeration to say that
no finer company of women were ever
seen in this or any other coutnry than
those who met us at the San Francisco
pier under the auspices of the Red Cros3
Society. The culture, refinement and
wealth of the city were there represent
ed. Besides the breakfast each man was
given a souvenir postal card and a pencil
to send a message home. Flowers were
given in such abundance that 1 ncle
Sam's soldiers looked more like high
school boys coming from their commence
ment exercises than men going to battle.
Tt was indeed, a memorable sight to see
bouquets of American Br-auy roses tucked
behind cartridge belts holding forty

ABSTRACTED SUPPLIES.
Quartermaster Sergeant of the
Twentieth Kansas Arrested
for Embezz'ement
Sergeant James A. Young, regimental
quartermaster sergeant of the Twentieth
Kansas Volunteers, was yesterday or
dered to his quarters to await trial before
the court martial now in session upon a
charge of embezzlement under the 60th
article of war.
It was discovered yesterday by Colonel
Funston that since Young took up tho
duties of his office he has been conduct-
Ing a prosperous business on his own ac
count at the expense of the Government.
Scarcely a day has passed that he has
not sent a wagon load of stuff abstracted
from the Government stores under his
charge down town in the delivery wagon
of a prominent grocery house. It has
been his scheme to take smoking and
chewing tobacco in exchange for the
stolen goods and dispose of it to the men
of the regiment.
As far as Young's peculations have been
investigated he is discovered to be over
*10J short in his stores.
Drill and Old Army Songs.
Next Saturday evening. In the Mechan
ics" Pavilion, will be given one of the
most unique military drills that has so
far taken place this summer. The lowa
regiment, which, along with its famous
band, so creditably distinguished it
self In its exhibition several weeks
ago, will appear. Besides the regimental
and battalion drills, two strikingly novel
features, which will be announced to
morrow, are on the programme. The
drilling will be interspersed with old
army songs, by a selected chorus, and a
promenade concert and drill will follow
until 12 o'clock. The affair will be under
the auspices of the San Francisco Lincoln
Relief Corps. General admission, 25 cents.
A few seats which are to be reserved will
be on sale at Sherman & Clay's, at 50
cents.

the troops now in camp at the Presidio
and at Camp Merritt. It would seem that
the nights of sleeping on the damp sand
are numbered.

under guard.

YOUNGSTERS DO BATTLE.
Lively Bouts at the San Francisco
Athletic Club Last Evening.
Two slashing six-round bouts and an
abundance of preliminary "goes" were
witnessed by a large number of fight fol
lowers at the San Francisco Athletic Club
last "night. Although little srore was
spilled there was enough stiff punching
to satisfy the most hardened ring-goers.
Jack Ennls and "Billy" Perry, two 125
pound youngsters of the popular club,
furnished the first interesting event.
Perry's height and reach favored him in
the first part of the bout, but Ennis'
hard punching evened matters. Perry
gained a lead in the first two rounds, but
after that Ennis showed to advantage.
The latter was a two-handed fighter, and
assuming the aggressive landed repeat
edly on his opponent. Perry was not at
all" backward and threw in some stiff
lefts which gained the approval of the
crowd. Both men showed willingness, and
blows rained hard and fast. At the con
clusion of the sixth round Referee Mc-
Laughiin declared the fight a draw.
In the bout between, Jack McMahon and
Fddie Toy, also 125-pound representatives
of the club the former was declared win
ner after six rounds of lively fighting.
McllÃhon showed superior science, while
Toy possessed grit and strength. This,
however did not avail Toy, as he was the
recipient of nearly all the blows. Judges
"Kid" Parker and Gus Koster favored
MoMahon. and he was given the decision.
The preliminary events were participat
ed in by Hogan and Dean, Whipple and
Dierks Ganfield and Wiley and Devine
and McDevitt.
It Was a Tie Vote.
The Supreme Court has approved thÂ«
judgment of the Superior Court of Tu
lare County in the contest of J. W.
Thomas and M. W. Smith for the office
of Supervisor of the Third District. Soon
after the last election the two candidates
appealed to the court, which found that
each had received the same number of
votes. However, the court gave judgment
in favor of Thomas, and the higher
court's decision is that thfrc is no rea
son for disturbing the judgment of tha
lower court.

WHERE STAMPS MUST GO.
Batch of Important Decisions From
the Treasury Department.
The following list of decisions relative
to the new stamp tax law was received by
Collector Lynch yesterday:
A 2-ccnt stamp is required on an order
for cash drawn on a merchant by one of
his customers.
Certificates of deposit drawing Interest,
if left a certain time, are taxable the
same as promissory notes.
If papers in the nature of receipts are
given In lieu of checks and are used as
commercial negotiable instruments they
are checks and not receipts, and are sub
ject tO tax.
Where certificates of stock are delivered
as collateral, the stock to be forfeited
only upon condition of failure to pay the
debt for which it is. pledged, a stamp is
required as a pledge and not as a sale.
Heal estate mortgage notes require to
be stamped In addition to the stamps
placed on the mortgage. ; -**Â.
Where there is a pledge of property ac
companying and promissory note, which
pledge Is subject to stamp tax under sched
ule A, this stamp tax must be paid, not
withstanding the fact luat-a stamp is also'
required on the note connected with it.
In cases of loans on real estate where
promissory notes are given, which are
not paid at maturity, but on which an
extension of time of payment is granted
without the taking of an ew note, it is
held that every such extension is a re
newal of the note within the meaning of
the statute, and that the. requisite stamp
must be affixed for every such renewal
or extension. This also applies to notes
discounted before July 1, falling due on
or after that date.
The person who signs and issues a bank
check without affixing the proper stamp
becomes involved in liability to penalties
under section 10 of the act, unless it is
W. H- STALLARD ar>d t\)2 Woman He Tried to Kill-
shown that he had no design to evade
the payment of the stamp tax. and that
the requisite stamp was affixed. and can
celed by the bank or person upon whom
It was drawn before payment.
BuCfcet-shop proprietors giving mem
orandum Of transactions are required to
P^h% P rc^t tt i a ri X ca a t SS e br oo OO f ker stoclc is sold and
stamn tax is paid on memorandum there
of upon transfer of this certflcato to
purchaser's name, no additional tax for
such transfer Is required. Where one cer
tificate represents several shares of stock
(howover large the number of shares) on
the transfer of this certificate the stamp
tax is to be reckoned on its face value
and not on the face value of each sepa

SOUGHT TO
TAKE HIS
WIFE'S LIFE
Cowardly Crime of W.
H. Stallard.
SHOOTS HIS VICTIM TWICE
THE TRAGEDY OCCURRED IN A
FIRST-STREET CAPE,
To Justify His Act the Would-Be
Assassin Seeks to Slacken the
Woman's Reputation â Not
Dangerously Hurt.
Infuriated at her refusal to endure his
brutality any longer, William H. Stallard
deliberately shot his wife twice, shortly
alter noon yesterday. The shooting took
place in the Royal Restaurant, 14 First
street, when the place was tilled with
patrons, and that the woman was not
killed is due to her assailant's poor
marksmanship and his seizure before he
could lire again.
The tragedy was the culmination of
many years of marital infelicity, owing
to the husband's excessive indulgence in
intoxicants, which had developed all the
latent brutality and viciousness of his
nature.
The couple were married in Denver in
1874, where Stallard was employed as
switchman In the yards of the Union
Pacific Company. They have one son, 23
years of age, who is still in Denver. For
a time the Stallards lived happily to
gether, but gradually he fell into evil
ways and began to maltreat his wife in
a shameful manner, while his conduct
toward his son became so intolerable that
the youth was compelled to leave home.
Two years ago Stallard's intemperate
habits caused his dismissal from the rail
road company's employ, and then he gave
himself up entirely to drinking 1 whisky
and beating the poor woman who was
struggling to earn a living for them both.
Not only did he kick and strike her al
most daily, but he would forcibly take
her earnings and purchase liquor.
So unendurable became the life she was
leading that Mrs. Stallard fled from the
brute a year ago last March, coming to
San Francisco. She had a married sis
ter residing at 624 Fourth street, and there
Mrs. Stallard took up her residence. She
secured employment In the Emporium
and lived in comparative peace and con
tentment until one day Stallard found her
out.
'.the usual promises of reformation were
made, the usual reconciliation ensued, and
Stallard and his wife began life anew in
the Fourth-street house. He obtained a
position as switchman in the Southern
I'acific yards, but hardly had he started
to work before he renewed his former
habits. And now he found a new source
of torment for his wife. About the time
Mrs. StaUard started for San Francisco
an old Denver neighbor, named Budd,
also came West, and Stallard frequently
accused his wife of having eloped with
him. Nobody knew the charge was base
less better than Stallard himself, yet he
would work himself into a frenzy over
what he termed his wife's pernd"*' and
vent his rage upon her in the most brutal
manner. Frequently Mrs. Stallard's
brother-in-law and other occupants of the
house found it necessary to interfere to
save her from her husband's violenc<>
Stallard again lost his position because
of his habitual drunkenness, and last
April ho returned to Denver, his wife
having refused to have anything more
to do with him. Mrs. Stallard soon after
resigned her position in the Emporium to
accept the position of forf-woman in a
factory at an increased salary, but she
only held the new position a few weeks
when her predecessor returned to work
and she was thrown out of employment.
Two months ago Mrs. Stallard obtained
a place in the overall factory of Neubauer
Urns at lh First street, but her pay was
meager, and she was obliged to give up
her rooms in the Fourth-street house,
going to board with some friends named
ftann at 1063 Tennessee street. Here she
found peace and quiet, and though her
work was hard and her compensation
barely sufficient to meet her modest
needs she struggled bravely and con
tentedly on, rarely leaving her home ex
cept to go to her employment
Last Monday, while busy at her work
the door opened and Mrs. Stallard
confronted by her recreant husband
Fvarful of a scone. Mrs. Stallard asked
him to step outside and when the door
had closed upon them the distressed
woman asked what he wanted. Stallard
first demanded that she take him bark
once more, but this she refused sa-dr^he
would never live with him again. Stallard
pleaded, but she remained firm in her de
termination to have nothing- more to do
with him Finally she returm-d to her
work and Stallard went away only to
return again on Tuesday. He' waited on
First street until Mrs. Stallard had fin
ished her day's work, and when she
emerged from the factory he accosted
her. They walked along together, board-
Ing an electric car, for Mrs. Stallard's
boarding place is in the Potrero. Stallard
renewed his Importunities to be taken
back once more, and upon his wife again
refusing became so violent and abusive
that several times the conductor threat
ened to eject him from the car. Stallard
continued on to the house on Tennessee
street, abusing the woman all the "way !
and adding to his old charge the state
ment that she was then living with a man :
named Haason, who also boarded with
the Ranns.
Stallard finally returned to the city and
yesterday, when Mrs. Stallard eft the
factory fc-r her noonday meal, she again
encountered the man at the foot of the
stairs. Stallnrd asked her to go to lunch
with him, saying it would be their last
meal together. Not daring to refuse,
Mrs. Statlard accompanied him into the
restaurant at 14 r irst street, but finding
tne tables nearly all full they went out.
Stallard wanted to go to another restau
rant, but his wife demurred, saying she
had only a half hour for lunch, and she
';new of no other place In the immediate
neighborhood. They re-entered the place
and seated themselves at next to the last
of the tables In the center row. The
waiter took their order, and just then a
woman seated herself at their table. Stal
lard asked his wife to change seats with
him, which she did. She thought it
strange, but it is evident that he did it
for the purpose of having a better chance
to use his right arm.
Stallard once more asked his wife to
live with him again, promising tv take her
back to Denver and provide a grioii home
for her. She replied that it would be
better for him to go back and prepare
the home first, and if she found lie was
sincere in his promise to reform she
might give him one more cnance.
â¢â Then you refuse to go with me, do
you, Emma?" he demanded, straighten
ing up in his chair.
"Yes; you must give some proof of your
reformation." she replied.
Like a flash Stullard drew a heavy re
volver from hia breast pocket and fired.
The bullet struck his wife in tne right
shoulder, close to the neck. It ranged
upward and out through her coat, crash-
Ing through a window in the front of the
restaurant. Mrs. Stallard jumped to her
feet, half turning as she d.- so. Stallard
fired again, the bullet striking the right
shoulder blade and ranging across the
back under the skin lodged over the left
shoulder blade. ,
Before Stallard could fire a third time
William Fitts, a brawny teamster .sitting
just across the aisle, grappled with BtaJ
lard. forcing his right arm above his
head. The would-be murderer, who is a
powerful man, struggled desperately, but
a dozen others came to the brave JMtts
assistance, and while R. B. Chandler dis
armed Stallard several others helped to
throw him to the floor.
Somebody shouted to hanpr the fellow,
and there were calls for a rope, etc. Had
not Policeman Ross arrived just then it
is likely that Stallard would never have
reached the City Prison, for he was in
the hands of a class of men who would
think little of hanging a cowardly wife
murderer.
Stallard was handcuffed and immedi
ately pretended to be exceedingly drunk,
disclaiming all knowledge of what he had
done and declaring he was crazed l<y
liquor. It was quite palpable that the fel
low was shamming, for he had not shown
the least sign of being intoxicated before
the shooting, but he Kept up the stupid
sham for hours after he was locked up in
the City Prison.
Meanwhile, Mrs. Stallard had been re
moved to the Harbor Receiving Hospital,
where the surgeons removed the second
bullet and found that neither wound was
of a dangerous nature. Her sister was
soon with her, and to-day she will be re
moved to her boarding place in the Po
trero.
Mrs. Stallard is about 40 years of age,
and is spoken of in the highest terms by
all who know her. The forewoman of the
factory where she is employed visited her
as soon as she could, and was loud in her
praise of the unfortunate woman, as were
also her former employers in the Empo
rium and the people with whom she
bfiards.
At first Stallard would not discuss the
shooting, pretending that he was too
drunk to know what had happened. He
rolled about In a most grotesque manner,
and it w; r all too apparent that he was
acting th' part with the object of evad
ing responsibility for his act Finally, to
a Call rpporter. Stallard said his wife had
eloped from Denver with J. J. Budd ovor
a year ago and that he had followed her
to this city. They had become reconciled,
but in April, last, he returned to Denver
to see his son. who was sick, and he got
back last Monday to find his wife living
with another man. Stallard could not be
induced to talk about the shooting, and
when pressed resumed his former atti
tude of helpless intoxication.
Those who know Mrs. tftal'ard best
scout the story of her elopement and the
other charge, and say that sj,;ch charges
by her husband are only more evidence
of his utter depravity.
In the presence of two detectives last
night Mrs. Stallard made the following
statement of the stabbing:
" I have been married to my husband
about twenty-four years. I have had
trouble with him ever since we were mar
ried. He has been so jealous and cruel
to me. I could not tell you the number
of times my life has been in danger of
him.
"About Christmas time my husband
assaulted me and T was confined to my
room in the Washington Hotel for some
time, the result of tho beating- he gave
me. I swore out a warrant for his arrest,
tut It was not served. I met my hus
band Tuesday about 3:30 p. m. on First
street. We walked along -First street to
Market, where we took a car. While on
the car he commenced to abuse me and
the conductor threatened to put him off.
He left me on the car about Bluxome
and Third streets and I continued until
I reached my home on Tennessee street.
A short time prior to the shooting my
husband came to where T worked and
sent one of the girl? to call me. I came
down and spoke to him.
"After a short conversation ho made me
promise to go to lunch with him. 1 then
left him, going upstairs to attend to my
work.
"As I emerped from the factory at
noon he was ptandinp outside, evidently
waiting for me. We went to the res
taurant in which the shooting took place.
He wanted me to go to Denver with him,
but I refused. Just then a woman, whose
name I do not know, came in and sat at
the same table.
"My husband at this juncture asked me
to change places with him. I did so, and
was surprised that he did not order any
thing but tea. I asked him why he had
not ordered something more substantial,
but he did not reply. A few minutes
later he again asked me If I would go to
Denver, and for the second time I replied
in the negative. He then drew his pistol
and shot me twice."

Further decline in Sliver.
Wheat lower and very soft.
Barley fairly steady.

Hay still arrives heavily.
No further advance in Beans.

Butter, Cheese and i^^f unchanged.
Another car of Eastern Poultry sold.

Prunes beginning to drop.
Provisions unchanged.
llid*-s weak at the decline.

A BANKER'S VIEW.
THE COMMERCIAL SITUATION AS SEEN
THROUGH NEW YORK EYES.
The New York circular of Henry Clews,
under date of July "" says: "The stock mar
ket Is Just new passing through the usual
period of midsiinniiT quiet and no marked
change la In immediate I 'â â -â  The outlook,
however, ii for a strong market. Weak spots
are few and difficult to find, and securities are
held with such confidence that the risks on
the "bear" side are altogether too great for
any ventures In that quarter in the absence of
unfavorable development*?* Only two possible
hlndranoea to a further "bull" movement are
worth considering: an outbreak of yellow fever
or complications with foreign powers arising
from the war. As a factor In the stork market
the war Is at much diminished importance be
cause of recent successes Nevertheless, delay la
not a favorable Influent and progress toward
peace is .julte likely tv be â  n xlgzag lines.
"Ultimately the war will prove a powerful
stimulus to the whole country. It has burled
old political Issues which were paralyzing
trade and Industry and developed In our people
a spirit of confidence and aggressiveness which
Wl 1 Inevitably l>Â« reflected In the business af
fairs of the nation The political situation is
more satis.'a-tory than for many years past.
New question* are tit'for? us, the solution of
â¢which will broaden and strengthen the whole
country from the Atlantic to the Pacific In
the financial situation there is also reason for
confidence. Money Is plentiful and promises
to continue so. Gold must come from Europe
In settlement of the â¢ â¢ Lord 1 nary trade bal
anco. and our supplies a:i' likely to br matr-
rlHlly Increased by shipment* from the Klon
dike. It is quite likely also that the banks
â will Increase their circulation to a considerable
extent when they have acquired .i suppply of
the new bonds; the placing of which as a
popular loan was h gratifying and significant
example ol the cnntlden<*<Â» of the people In the
Government. The moral effect of this method
was excellent and w>rtn its cost to the Gov
ernment, Which could readily have syndicated
the whole issue at a more profitable price.
"The agricultural situation is another and
very Important element of promise. Farmers
a:-- rai.uiily getting r.ut of debt, and with In
creased prosperity are buying more freely of
both the necessities and luxuries of life. The
better prices for whsat 'an the distribution
of many additional millions in the West and a
larger traffic for the railroads, both eastbound
and westbound, for some time to come.
Western merchants coming to this city are
enthusiastic In their reports of trade prospects
durincr the coming fall, and the outlook is
lhet the next six months -fill show a very de
cided business revival Even In the South

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. July 27.â Nearly all the
â trength and activity in to-day's stock markets
were confined to the first hour. At the open
.ng there was a rush to buy. as a result of

tnorninK on the announcement that Spain had
iddresse.l the United States Govtrnment with
L request of peace. This early demand was fed
quite freely by realizing sales which held

last night and even dragged them below the
last night's level. Hut these offerings were
quite confidently and aggressively absorbed,
an 1 the demand was still unsatisfied. The
market was apparently embarked on a sus-
talned upward movement when sugar began to
pause after an advance of nearly 2 points,
which resulted In dislodging heavy covering
orders. Very large blocks of the stock, many
of them of 10,000 shares and upwards, were
taken on the advance. Hut after touching 141
support seemed to be withdrawn and a steady
stream was poured upon the market for the
rest of the day. Several times the price
steadied and rallied slightly, only to make a
renewed downward plunge, each successive de
cline dislodging heavy stop loss orders. The
price dropped over 4 points from the top Â»nd
rallied only feebly at the close. The slu.np was
coinpanled by rumors of unloading for inside
Interests with a view to retirement. The de
moralization in sugar unsettled the whole list
Â»nd put a stop to the upward movement for the
rebt of the day. The decline Jin the general
market was desultory and was not accom
panied by heavy offerings. It was very ob
vious, however, that professional traders
realized to a large extent at the opening on
peace news, and they probably viewed with
complacency the subsequent reaction, offering
opportunity to buy back stock at lower level,
declines from the best reached a point or over
In moat of the prominent stocks and net losses
In some cases are as much as a point. The an
nouncement during the day of the engagement
Df a small amount of gold In London for im
port had no effect. The transaction was ! re
tarded as of a special character, exchange not
let being considered at the gold import point.

LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK. July 27â The Evening Post's
London financial cablegram Fays: The stork
markets here continue stagnant. Investment
stocks being dull on the rise in money rates
and the continued foreign drain of cold. Ameri
cans opened good. The commencement of sold
exports to New York attracted much attention.
Trices closed under the best. At present the i
I. Id operators are the Canadian banks, but It j
Is understood that New York is the destination
of the metal.
Spanish 4s rose to 40 on the peace prospects,
but closed nearly, a point under trie best.
Gold has been engaged here to-day for export
to the United States, being taken both from
i he open market and from the bank. The dis
. count rate hap risen In consequence 5-16 to l'<%
rer cent.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, July 27.â 'With a slump at Liver
pool and favorable weather, Wheat started at a
decline. September, which closed yesterday at
65% c, opened at from Â«5@65%c, and settled to
64% c before all the early offerings could be
absorbed. July started at %c decline, the first
sale being at 70c, and before It did better It
sold another lc â  lower, selling at 69c. A de
mand for a small quantity of July soon dl
\ vulged how little was for Rale upon the appear -
I ance of any legitimate demand. From 63c there
was an Immediate Jump to 71% c. Trading
sentiment was bearish and every one seemed
to be on the selling side. About the only de
mand was from shorts, who had a satisfactory
profit and bought In on the decline to secure
It. The export Inquiry was aroused to greater
activity by the decline In price. New York
reported a considerable quantity of gold going
to the Continent. This caused a slight rally
near the close, but partly failed of effect on
account of a decline at , Antwerp r.t 2%@5
centimes reported on enormous yields through
out the Continent. â  September - closed %@%c
lower and December %<y%c down.
The heaviness of Wheat was felt In the Corn
pit and had a tendency to depress the latter.
Generous rains fell In various . sections, but the
weather was still very dry and hot -in some
regions of the corn-growing country. The gen
eral sentiment was bearish and Indication)! of
unloading by a prominent long started the
entire fraternity in a similar proceeding. Sep
tember left off %c lower.
Oats were firm early, but later sagged with
Corn. September lout %@%c on the day.
Outside buying started Provisions rather
steady. Then prices collapsed on the decline
In the other grain markets. Commission houses
sold freely. Pork declined 25c and lard and
ribs 15c each.

The weekly report of the Weather Bureau
nays that fruit la suffering- from the drynesa of
the Boil and that it Is turning out small In size
anil dropping from the trees. This condition
will get worse Instead of better as the summer
advances. I

especially colored, are showing- signs of doing
better. Apricots arcs also firm. Figs are In
rather better supply. Melons are about the
same. Supplies of Berries are moderate and
they are llrm. Pears are steady.
Muscat Grapes from Fresno sold at $1 per
box.

DRIED FRUITS. NUTS, RAISINS, ETC.
The reports from different sections of the
State to the Weather Bureau show that fruit
Is showing the evil effects of the dryness of the
ground and Is not only running small In size,
but Is dropping from the trees. This applies
particularly to Prunes, and confirms what The
Call has already said.
The market Is dull and featureless.

THE STOCK MARKET.
Mining stocks were rnthÂ»r firmer again yes
terday and several scored a slight advance.
The San Francisco Board has abolished the
admission charge to the lobbies and will here
after admit male dealers free.
Local securities were active and Hutehlnson
advanced to $61.
The yield of the Overman mine for the past
week amounted to 16 carloads of ore, the aver
age assay value of which was ?S2 78 per ton.
This ore was extracted from the north drift
workings on the 90y-fnot level.
In the Caledonia mine on the 1000-foot level |
the lateral drift to the westward at the head j
of the Incline upraise from the 1100-foot level !
southeast drift is out 34 fort, 8 feet having j
been added for the week. The formation Is
quartz of a low assay value.

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the Unlud States Hydrographlc
Office, located In the Merchants' Exchange, Is
maintained In Pan Francisco for the benefit of
mariners without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail-
Ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information enn always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of Interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new FÂ»rry
building, at the foot of Market street, is hoisted
about ten mlnuteE before noon and dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic sljjnal re
ceived each day from the I'nited States Signal
ObFervatory, Mare Island. Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error. If any, is published
In tha morning papers th^ following day.
CHAS. P. WELCH.
Ensign (retired), U. S. N., In charge.

NOTE! â In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the ljft
hand column ami the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day. the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column glvÂ»s the
last tide of the dny, except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
I'nited States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is the mean of the lower low waters.

pany may apply for an injunction re

"Why, think of itâ the fifth city of

ceive when they go for a drive along Mar
ket street.
"1 am very glad, indeed, that the Board
of Supervisors has acted decisively, and
I sincerely hope that the resolution will
be sustained by the court. I presume the
railway company's attorneys wll dig up
an innumerable number of technicalities
and endeavor in every way possible to ob
struct the much needed reform, and op
pose the wishes of all our citizens."
"How long will It take you to tear up
the outer track from Second to Sixth
street?" asked tho reporter.
"In less than half a day." replied Mr.
Ambrose. "When the time comes for me
to act there wil be no delay. I will put
a large force of men on tho job, and when
they get at those rails thoy wll come up
in a hurry. I have consulted my attorney
and shall be governed by his opinion. He
has been looking up tho statutes for sev
eral days and will advise me to-day."

ROOM FOR CONVENTIONS
Sacramento Citizens Mnke Pro
vision for the Dele
gates.
Arrangements have been perfected
making it possible to hold both the Demo
cratic and Republican State conventions
in the new pavilion building at Sacra
mento. This result was achieved yester
day by the committee of Sacramento citi
zens having the matter in charge, and
the fact was immediately communicated
to the State committees of both parties.
The news was received with much satis
faction, as it gave assurance that the
conventions will not be crowded for room.
and that the sessions can thus be held
with as t-mall amount of personal dis
comfort as the usual midsummer climate
of the capital city will permit.
It was given out some time ago that
the conventions would have to be held
!n the old pavilion building at Sixth and
M streets, for the reason that the new
pavilion would be in use by those who
are preparing exhibits for the State Fair,
which opens during the first week in Sep
tember. The floor space in the old build
ing Is somewhat limited, and the proposi
tion to hold the conventions there
brought forth a storm of protests that
threatened to injure the reputation of
Sacramento as a convention city. The
citizens' committee accordingly set to
work and succeeded in giving intending
exhibitors such assurances as resulted in
the surrender of the new pavilion to the
conventions until August 23.

They were slow in getting together.

tained many politicians, who dropped in

Chairman Friend stated that the com

State Central Committee, and Secretary

Mr. Friend announced that the only
thing to be done was the ftcing of the
time and r>iace for the convention and

Mr. Luchslr.ger of Solano su~<rested

â whether the delegates to the Congres
sional convention should be the same as

The place of meeting called forth quite

land, suggesting tna.t as Alameda County
had C 7 delegates she had the greatest

of Glenn expressed the idea that Oakland

tiy his proxy. Edgar, placed Martinez In

anil Sacramento two.
The date was made Saturday, August
20. at 11 a. m., on motion of Mr. Luch
singer, by a unanimous vote.
The question of apportionment of dele
gates caused another discussion. Mr.
Morrison moved that it be the same as

each 200 votes for Elector McKinfay. but
none at large. Mr. Kendricks moved to
base the apportionment on Htlborn'a vote,
and that delegates at large be allowed

"I don't think Alameda County should

stitute to the prior motions he moved that

ing elected its delegates, the action was
ratified. '
The county chairmen will be required to
send In returns from the primaries or
caucuses for the election of delegates so
that they may be in before the ISth.
No formal recommendation was made
to the county committees regarding to
date of primaries, although it was sug
gested that they be held the same time

MERRIT MAY NOW BE
GOVERNOR OF MANILA
NEW YORK, July 27.â A Tribune
special from Washington says: Gen
eral Merritt has reached Manila, ac
cording to the most conservative calcu
lation of the War Department officials,
and it is believed that he has already
assumed the military governorship of
