Â£>ewey of his readiness to surrender the
moment the American forces are pre
pared to receive the* city.
MISS HAINES
NOT MURDERED
The Autopsy Disclosed
No Trace of Poison.
SUSPICION NOW AT REST
THE WOMAN'S DEATH DUE TO
NATUBAL CAUSES.
Chnrges of Underhand Work Made
During Excitement Are Retract
ed and the Trouble Is
Over.
BERKELEY, July 27.â Mrs. D. Allison,
whoso name was seriously Implicated by
rumors regarding the poisoning of Miss
Mabel Haines, is now entirely free from
the grave charge Indirectly preferred
against her by the mother of the unfortu
nate girl. Mr. Allison as soon as he
found that Mrs. Haines harbored suspi
cions concerning his wife's action
Insisted upon a post-mortem examina
tion. This Coroner Baldwin granted and
ordered the autopsy to be held this morn
ing. As a result the accusations made
last night are proved to be totally un
founded.
Drs. Payne, Rowell and Gladding, who
conducted the examination at 10:30 o'clock
this morning, were at first disposed to in
cline toward the theory that carbolic acid
had been taken, as the color and condi
tion of the dead girl's lips seemed to show
unmistakable evidence of the use of the
acid. But a thorough investigation failed
to disclose any traces whatever of car
bolic acid or any other poison. This con
clusion was reached by the physicians
shortly before noon, and was submitted
to the Coroner's Inquest, held this even
ing.
The apparent mystery surrounding Miss
Haines' sudden death, and the fact that
it was at first associated with the murder
of Edgar Halsey by Stacey Rodgers some
months ago, is accordingly all cleared up.
The Halsey affair did not occur at the
house in which Miss Haines died but at
her brother's residence, some blocks
away. The shooting in that case was
over the domestic employed at the house,
and did not concern any memoer of the
Haines family.
Dr. Allen's ground for signing the cer
tificate of the death of Miss Halnea was
confirmed by the doctors who conducted
the post mortem examination this morn
ing. They reported that death was due
to natural causes. Miss Haines is known
to have been subject to attacks of vio
lent vomiting, and it was during one of
these that a blood vessel in the brain
was ruptured, causing apoplexy, which
brought about the Quick and fatal re
sult.
It is understood that Mrs. Haines has
withdrawn the charges which first started
the suspicion that poison had been use 3.
Overcome with grief at arriving home to
find her daughter unconscious, and be
yond recovery, and noticing the smell
of carbolic acid In the room, the frantic
mother uttered remarks which brought
about the strained relations between her
and Mrs. Allison. Upon the decision of
the physicians conducting the autopsy,
that no" traces of the acid could be. found,
all ground for any accusation was taken
away, and the charges, made in the grief
and despair of the moment, were re
tracted.
The funeral of the deceased took place
this afternoon from the family residence
at the corner of Russell and Lorlna
streets. The Coroner's inquest held this
evening resulted in the verdict that death
was due to cerebral hemorrhage, caused
by an abscess on the brain.

CLAIMS THE IRENE WAS
ON A HUMANE MISSION
Prince Henry's Version of That Un
pleasant Incident at Subig
Bay.
BERLIN, July 27.â A dispatch from
Shanghai to-day says an official state
ment from Prince Henry of Prussia in re
gard to the Irene incident at Subig Bay,
Philippine Islands, has been published in
the Ost Asiatische Lloyd. It says the
Irene went to Subig Bay to take oft some
Spanish women and children who were in
distress. At Isla Grande the German
warship happened to meet a steamer be
longing to the insurgents, which left
without difficulty arising. On returning
the Irene met outside Manila Bay two
United States ships which did not speak
her. In conclusion the statement sets
forth that the removal of women and
children was "effected from motives of
humanity and with strict observance of
the rules of neutrality."

LIEUTENANT CAPRON
Body Escorted to Arlington Cemetery
by a Company of the Fifteenth
Pennsylvania.
WASHINGTON. July 27.â Funeral serv
ices over the remains of Lieutenant AHyn
Capron, one of the gallant heroes of
Roosevelt's Rough Riders, who fell before
Slboney July 1. were held here to-day, the
body having been brought north on the
Government transport Hudson. Services
were held in St. Paul's Episcopal Church.

sang "Nearer, My God, to Thee," and
"Lead, Kindly Light." The body was
then taken to Arlington, escorted by 100
men of the Fifteenth Pennsylvania Reg
iment, where i\ was Interred. Lieutenant
Capron's mother and widow were present,
but his father is with his battery of ar
tillery in Cuba.

Ex-Assemblyman Bretz Gives a Brief
Prophecy on the Meeting to
Be Held To-Morrow
Night.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway. July 27.
The new Populist County Central Com
mittee will meet In Judge Cliffs court
room to-morrow evening for the pur
poses of permanently organizing and
transacting auch other business as may
be brought up for consideration.
There promises to be a warm time over
the chairmanship. D. T. Fowler is tem
porary chairman and at the time he was
selected he was in favor of coalition; he
said that it would be suicidal to put up
an independent ticket, and made compli
mentary references to Judge Maguire. He
also participated in the famous caucus at
Sacramento. Since his nomination by the
bolters for Shanahan's running mate, he.
has taken a natural turn of mind, and
the out-and-out Maguire "Pops" are anx
ious to see F. J. Gregory elected per
manent chairman.
Gregory has no hesitancy in declaring
that he would be justified, under the cir
cumstances, in accepting the chairman
ship.
"My election would be a vindication of
my position," said Mr. Gregory to a Call
reporter to-day, "and a fitting rebuke to
such men as Bretz, who are not in har
mony with the best interests of the party.
Most assuredly, I look for a harmonious
meeting. The bolters cannot get control
of the committee, and, of course, thev_
cannot consistently remain in our camp.
The bolters will make every effort to
capture the chairmanship for J. C. Estey.
''If we have the majority Estey will be
our man," said ex-Assemblyman Bretz,
"but if the Buckley push and 'Mollie
Magulres" hold the majority, Gregory will
get it. We will leave and meet in my
office in the old Moffitt block, where we
will permanently organize as the only
Populist County Committee. The fusion
lsts stole our convention at Sacramento.
Two State Supreme Courts have decided
that a fusion ticket is not partisan and
must go on by petition and partake of a
new name.
"The meeting to-morrow night will
probably result In a separation," con
cluded Mr. Bretz. "All that the forces
can do will be to get divorced."
The county committee, by Assembly
districts, consisting of thirty-seven mem
bers, is as follows, and it will take nine
teen to elect officers:

R. Lyle. L. J. Van Horn; Forty-seventhâ

Jones. J. E. Taylor. R. B. Myers; Forty
eighthâF. J. Gregory, A. M. Stoddard.

I. D. Shepherd; Forty-ninthâ Robert

MYSTERIOUS YOUNG
HURT IS LOCATED
HE SHOWS HIS FATHER TO BE
A FALSIFIER.
He Is a Soldier in a Nebraska Regi
ment and May Go to Porto
Rico.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway. July 27.
Mrs. Alfonso Schaefer received a letter
from her long-absent son, Fred Hurt, to
day, that relieved her mind of a mother's
solicitude and at the same time gives
proof that J. J. Hurt, her divorced hus
band, did not tell the truth when he an
nounced in open court that he had re
ceived a telegram from the young man
stating that he was In the battles of Juno
23 and 24 before Santiago.
It was during the hearing of the peti
tion of Hurt for the removal of the three
minor children from their mother's cus
tody that Hurt exclaimed dramatically
in court: "Your son has enlisted in the
army, and I received a telegram Just be
fore I left home from him. in which he
eaid that he had taken part in the assault
before Santiago and escaped injury."
This Mr*. Schaefer declared was done
to annoy and worry her, and it accomr
plished its purpose until this morning,
when she received a letter from her ab
sent son. He is in Camp Thomas, Chick
amauga, Ga., and has not been to Cuba.
He enlisted in Company H, Second Regi
ment, Nebraska Volunteer Infantry, at
Chadron, Nebr., on May C, and on the 19th
left there for Camp Thomas, where they
have been ever since.
Young Hurt did not know where his
mother was located, so wrote a letter and
inclosed it in another to his aunt in Ore
gon, to have forwarded, and asked her to
write him. He declared his desire to
reach Cuba instead of coming home.
REASON FLED WHEN
HER HUSBAND LEFT
PITIABLE CONDITION OF A 20-
YEAR-OLD BRIDE.
Thrown Upon the Sands of Strangers
After Having Been Abandoned
by Her Legal Protector.
OAKLAND, July 27.â Mrs. Jessie
Bronko. a bride of four months and only
20 years of age, is confined in the Receiv
ing Hospital to-night, with her reason
dethroned. She and her husband came
here only three months ago from Fall
River, Mass., expecting to find work, but
fate seemed against them. Recently her
husband deserted her, leaving her penni
less. She threatens suicide and murder in
her insane fancies.
Mrs. Bronko has a pitiable history. Born
in Portugal, she came to this country
when a mere child, and was married at
14 Her first husband died, leaving her
with an infant to support. The child is
now with relatives in the East.
No doubt the mother's mourning over
the forced separation, added to her other
troubles, hastened her mental collapse.
Mr and Mrs. Bronko have been living at
716 Filbert street of late. He was re
ported to the police to-night as having
been seen on the street, threatening to
commit suicide, but nothing tangible can
be found to-night of his whereabouts.

OAKLAND, July 27.â The Enquirer

"To-day Division Superintendent James
A Philippine Heroine.
One of the Philippine insurgent leaders la a

HPO people in this country who live so long that
their lives seem charmed also, hut the only
charm about It Is that they keep up their
strength aud vitalize their blood with that cele
brated remedy, lies tetter's Stomach Bitters.

BOMBARDED BY TWO
AMERICAN WARSHIPS
Tunas de Saza Shelled and Spaniards
Beturn the Fire â Blanco Gives
a Swell Banquet.
Special cable to The Call and the New York
Herald. Cupyrightod. 189S, by James Gor
don Bennett.
HAVANA, July 27.â Two American
warships yesterday bombarded Tunas
de Saza. The Spanish artillery an
swered fire.
General Blanco to-night gave a ban
quet in honor of the foreign military
attaches and members of his general
staff. The commander of the French
warship d'Estalng, now in the harbor,
was invited to the banquet and so were
the foreign consuls. On account of ill
ness the French Consul was unable
to attend. General Blanco was assist
ed in receiving his guests by General
Arolas, Military Governor of Havana.
During the banquet the army band
played.
SPANISH TRANSPORTS
SAIL FOR SANTIAGO
Embarkation of Prisoners for Home
Is Expected to Begin in a
Few Days.
WASHINGTON, July 27 âIt is ex
pected that the embarkation of the
Spanish prisoners will begin very soon.
The Alicante sailed yesterday from
Martinique and the hospital ship San
Augustine sailed to-day. The Isla
Luzon and the Isla Panaya sailed
from Cadiz to-day for Santiago. The
Leon XIII, the Salrustigen, the Cavo
donja and the Colon, all at Cadiz, are
expected to sail very soon. The capac
ity of these several ships is 14,830 men.
It will be observed that nope of these
ships were in Mexican ports, as had
been reported.

JAPANESE MINISTER

Herald. Copyrighted. 1898, by James Gor
don Bennett.
LIMA, Peru, July 27.â President Plerola
received the Japanese Minister in public
this afternoon. There was an immense
crowd present. The Minister made a
speech in Spanish.

NEWTON, Mass.. July 27.â Mrs. Albert
Bowker, the originator of and for twenty
two years president of the Women's
Board of Missions, died at her home here
to-day.

LACK OF TEAM WORK EVIDENT
THERE IS GREAT HARM IN
TAKING CHANCES.
Players Should Sacrifice When the
Situation Demands It, and Do
the Proper Thing at the
Right Time.
BY JOE CORBETT.
Tn reviewing last Sunday's contest be
tween the "Florists" of San Jose and the
Oaklands, I want to state that the game,
from a spectator's standpoint, was in
deffi a grand oik-, and from a strictly
basc-baJl view disclosed many Instances
of lack of tpa.m work In the field, but
principally at the bat; and also divulg.-d
the fact th-u some players are exceed
ingly careless In critical plays, through
no fault of theirs in one cuse, as I will
explain later, and also that they are
either Ignorant of the proper thing to do
at tn e proper time or care not, and, la-"t of
â¢Hi t! â  I them are detleient In the
Indispensably necessary art of sacrificing
or bunting.
Baseball being such an uncertain game,
1 dare not conjecture what might nave
occurred bad some of the plays I will
touch upon been attempted, especially
tbose where a batsman's cunning and
Judgment should be a, wirent. And I am
â  igly of the opinion that had that
game been properly played at the bat it
never would have passed the ninth in
ning.
I cannot deny exultation that it did,
however, for the reason that mistakes
and misplays were brought to light that
might have rested intact all season, and
an opportunity of correcting them done
likewise.
Tho most palpable mistake of the whole
game was the play of "Truck" Esan,
which gave the Oaklands their first run!
and. as it afterward turned out, the
game, as they did not tcore again until
the eleventh inning, while the Florists
did in the eighth. This error of Trurk
should never have occurred, md this
pravates the situation extreme
ly, as does the realisation that the play
In question should never nave come up.
V. Jth v man at second and third and I
think, one on first, Kent threw to third
avorlng to catch that runner nap
p;ng. His attempt proved unsuccessful
b rt Md larthy took the ball and threw It to
second, when there was not the slightest
possibility- of catching the runner at that
base.
He should never have thrown the ball.
't It Is i â  ' â  to to6B the sphere
around with ir.m on bases at any time
especially so when a chance to catch one
of them hardly offers Itself. The ball
however, came aJmost directly into this
eccentric Egan's hands, who, instead of
jetting both of them out to intercept it
only used one and missed it. Before it
was recovered the man on third had
crossed the plate with Oakland's first
run. McCarthy In the fin place 6hould
never have tossed the ball in that direc
tion, for, as I said before, there was no
-t unity to consummate a play.
Egan's mistake, however, was no fault
Of McCarthy's. It was horribly stupid
of him not to have put two hands to the
hall, and every player in the league
should profit by tne mistake. Don't for
gr'-odnesa' sake put one hand to a ball
when it Is possible to put two. I spoke
about this some time ago. and now that
it is obvious what serious results some
times attend this overindulgence in fancy
piaying. reform from being guilty of such
a gross mistake and do not be insensible
to its effects.
In this same inning on the Oakland side
three or four more mistakes occurred, all
of which wt-rc a direct deviation from the
elmp!i>st of baseball rules, and one in par
ticular, had it been attempted, end there
can be no excuee as to why It should not,
vr.ight have given a different aspect to
the entire game.
With a man on first and sf-cond and no
one out Bliss came to bat and. instead of
sacrificing, so as to enable those runners
to rf-ach third and second, respectively,
he trl<Hl to knock the cover off tho sphere,
end in consequence accomplished noth
ing except that he put himself out on a
fly to left field.
When it is palpably obvious that a team
is not hitting a pitcher often and safe
and that runs are very scarce there Is
no better game than the bunting and sac
rificing: game. And when there 1b a run
ner on first and one on second with no
one out It is always a good play to ad
rance the runners, no matter what tr.r>
pcore or situation.
In the case of Bliss there was but one
play for him to make on that occasion,
nr,<l it was to advance the runners. The
. the situation, everything called for
Kuch a play, yet he took that chance too
often Indulged In by the safest players,
and obtained the almost inevitable" re
sult
Had ho been fortunate enough to have
made a sinKic in such an exigency, while |
the play would be laudable, it would not j
be baseball, for under the circumstances
there was only one piny to mako, and it !
â was to bunt or sacrifice. Had he done
thie the chances of the game being won |
to that inning wore excellent. With men i
on second and third and but one out the i
Fltuatlon would necessitate the opposing
lnfleld playing in, and at Bucb a tlrre the j
chances for a base hit arc never better.
The next man up did not ninsrle. but
with that infield playing in he might have. ;
as hie chances for so doing were 50 per
cent better. Your rendition of the play
was not proper. Bliss, and as you were
told to bunt by nearly every player in
the team, you should have done so, and,
In fact, your own Judgment should have
prompted you as to what was the proper
thing.
Players si ould know when and when
not to sacrifice, and when they neglect
to do so in a situation that remands it, j
the welfare of the team, as a team, is '
not taken into consideration, and It I
Hhould be at all times.
The Baltimore team was successful be- I
cause every one of thorn, pitchers In- j
eluded, knew whnt to do in every emer- i
gency, ;ind worked only for the success I
of the club, collectively, Individual rec
ords going for naught. And to give an
Illustration <>f how devoted those boys
were to the Buccess of the team, as a
team. I saw Keeler deliberately sacrlllce
lust year when Clnrk of the. Louisvllles i
was but a few points behind him in the !
race for champion batter. He realized j
â¢what an honor it was to lead the league i
In batting, yet. with him. it would have I
been an empty honor had he not played
for the team's success, and sacrificed j
when the situation called for it.
Players should, therefore, think of the
team's success, and when an occasion de- i
manda a bunt or sacrifice, don't bo ufraid
to do so. Of source, to hit the bail feels
better, but the. proper play at the proper
time does more certain execution.
I cannot refrain from adverting to
another episode which occurred whiii '
Bliss was at the plate. Every one of the I
Oakland's team, particularly O'NeU waÂ« !
gesticulating in a manner -wild and ter- i
rlble, with the intention, no doubt of I
conveying to him the idea that he muot
bunt.
That is poor team work on thÂ« bench.
If you have anything, to say to a batter,
tell It to him before lie goes to the plate..'
If you want him to bunt, don't ten the
audience, the opposing team and the
Btatue on the City Hall about It. Kuch
open methods will defeat the effects of
any good play, as it enables the rival
team to offset the play, which can easily
be accomplished by a changing of posi
tion.
Be discreet at all times. Don't lot an i
opponent know what your intentions are. j
Keep him guessing as much as possible I <
the Fame as a pitcher does a batsman.'
Just imagine what a slaughtering pitch- :
ors would receive If they told batters the I
kind of a ball to expect. The same holds
good if batters make known their inten
tions. O'Netl told Bliss to bunt. I saw
him do bo, and so did the entire San I
Jose team. McCarthy, at third, came in
eloee and the whole infield was prepared
to take care of that bunt. Bliss fooled
them by hitting the ball, which would
have been a good move had not the situa
tion demanded a bunt or sacrifice. There
should be more team work on the bench.
It la not my intention to give the im
pression that Biles was playing for him
self and not the team. This would be do
ing him a great injustice. He, like a
great many players, thought the chances
for a hit excellent, and aico paid some
attention to the fact that if successful his
team would profit by the play. He made
a mistake, and even if he had made ft
safe hit I should criticise the play, for it
was not baseball.
I would rather see a team lose In en
deavoring to p.ay the (fame as it should
be played than see one win through tac
tics not baseball.
Another Instance which bespoke lack of
team work on the part of the Oaklands
was the manner Sullivan and Bliss went
after a foul fly in the vicinity of first
base. Either one of them could have
caught the ball, yet because there was a
lacking of team work it was permitted
to go safe.
When a ball is hit in the air the player
nearest it or who Intends taking it should
ejaculate in a loud and ringing manner.
"I have It," or In the parlance of the de
mand, "I got It." His cohorts should an
swer in an equally vociferous manner.
"Take It." In this way players will not
collide, and halls that should be caught
will not fall to the ground within a few
feet of players untouched, as occurred in
the case of Sullivan and Bliss.
Another instance of this sort occurred
between O'Neil and Donovan. They both
went after a fly ball and collided, but
fortunately Donovan had presence of
mind enough to hold the ball. There can
be no reason why these collisions should
creep into a game. And they are occa
sionally attended with very serious re
sults, as only a week ago a player back
East, in one of the minor leagues, col
lided with another aim shortly afterward
died from the injuries received. This
should be ai: awe-Inspiring lesson to all
players and convince them that team
work is necessary' If for n0 other reason
than the preservation of life.
I mentioned in the beginning of my ar
ticle that players are exceedingly careless
in critical plays, through. In some cases,
no fault of theirs. I spoke of Epan's in
excusable infringement, and I shall not
touch on another play, which. I am
nfraM, will not be recollected, for the
reason that it seemed and was a perfect
play.
However, I am speaking now of tho
carelessness of players who do not real
ize that they are so. The play In ques
tion occurred toward the latter part of
the gi'.nic. Hi ley singled to left field,
Mnsklman fielded the ball perfectly,
threw to second and there the play ter
minated. The way Mosklman stopped
the ball caused a chill to pass over ray
entire frame. Instead of getting in front
of it so as to be in position to block Its
course should it escape his hands, as
grounders generally do In the outfield, he
turned his head and body aside, rind cap
tured the ball without a semblance of a
doubt. It was an awful chance to take.
Had the sphere changed its course an
inch or mo?e it would, in all probability,
have evaded him and continued to the
fence, as it was a sharp hit.
In such a close game no kind of a
i chance Bhould be taken. Moskiman
! Bhould not have taken It. but should hava
j thought only of stoppi: ;the ballâ it made
! no difference whether he picked up cl*an
nr not â as there wus plenty of time to re
i cover and hold Hanley at first. I men
, tlon thi* incident only to impress upon
i players the necessity of being careful in
critical situ.itons.
Bran's negligence I have described, and
it should be a lesson to players who do
! not realize tho importance of being sure.
1 Mosklman completed his play, as he sup
i posed, in the usual proper manner, and
: is. therefore, nut :it fault. There is, how
ever, room for Improvement, and it is
never too late to take advantage of the
: fact.

Old Sol Beams on Fresno.
FRESNO, July 27.â This was the warm
est day of the season, and equaled the
highest temperature since the establish
ment of the Weather Bureau station in
this city. The maximum temperature as
announced by the Weather Bureau was
114 degrees. It was 112 yesterday. The
high temperature was rendered niore op
pressive by a hot wind.

A STUBBORN BUTCHER.
Fight Between the Police and a
Wealthy Butcher on McAllis
ter Street.
Fred Junker is a wealthy butcher and
has a store on McAllister street, near
Buchanan. He is determined to lay a
wooden sidewalk in front of his store,
but the police are just as determined that
he will obey the ordinance.
He commenced to lay the sidewalk on
Sunday, July 17. early In the morning,
but was arrested arid booked at the City
Prison. He gavo SlO cash ball and was
released. Four hours later he a.galn at
tempted to lay the sidewalk, and was
again arrested, this time putting up $20
bail.
The cases were called yesterday, and
â¢were continued till to-morrow. While
the defendant was in court his son, Fred
Junker Jr., attempted to lay the side
walk, and was promptly arrested by
Policeman Dunn. He was released on
giving $10 bail. Ills u>ze has also been
aspiirned to Judge Mogan'.s court.
Since Sunday, July 17, the police have
been compelled to keep an officer on
watch at Junker's shop to prevent him
from laying the sidewalk, so that his
fight against the ordinance is putting the
city to needless expense.
Ilobbed While He Slept.
William Rogers was arrested yesterday
aftt-rr.oon on a charge of grand larceny,
preferred against him by John Ross. Rog
ers is accused of abstracting $10 from the
pockets of Mr. Ross while the latter whs
taking his afternoon siestaon the benches
in the plaza.
Advances made on furniture and pianos, with
or without removal. J. Noonan. 1017-1023 Mission.

CERTAIN IT WAS THE
WORK OF A MISCREANT.
I am fully convinced that the fearful catastrophe of this morning was due
to the act of a fiend who wanted to destroy our property. I have carefully
Inspected the piece of fuse that has been found, and I am confident that
It was attached to a percussion cap and that It was this cap that was first
heard. This was evidently placed under the blow cask, ami the effect of its
explosion, a few seconds later, was to explode the blow tank and cause the
first explosion that was heard outside tho works. P earing fire, such, as
follows nearly every explosion, we ordered out the men and started to
clear away the wreck. As they were working they discovered the fuse, and
then came the second explosion that resulted so fatally.
I cannot conceive why anybody should want to harm us, but I feel sure
that such was the case, I do not believe in any fool Etory about a Spanish
spy, but it Is likely that there may be a traitor In our mlast. It is very
clear that it was not his intention to kill anybody, for he took a time for
his misdeed when no one was near enough to have been Injured. He elm
ply wanted to destroy property.
The second explosion was caused by some of the material from tho first
settling on some of the filters. These filters are shallow, zinc-lined troughs,
and they contain bags of common salt, through which the nitrate percolates.
A little sediment remains, and this, in connection with the stuff blown
about by the first explosion, caused the second accident. The nitro-glycer
lne house was closed down yesterday, and we did not intend to open it for
work until we had put in some new machinery. It was nearly empty, and
certainly not more (nan thirty pounds of explosive was contained lii the
whole house. Tho explosion is the smallest we have ever had during the
eighteen years we have been, here, but, unfortunately, the damage to em
ployes is greater than usual. W. R. QUINAN, Superintendent.
I have mÃ'le a careful lnvrptipatlon, and so far everything points to
: the work of to-day is that of a murderer. I have gone
â¢ men, but when all an- In nurh a state of nervousness it Is difll
any evidence of crime in any particular person. There is
never any use f.ir fuse in the works, and the only reason I can think for its
here la that it was brought here for base purposes. The fuse is now
lon of the Coroner, and I shall pursue my investigations along
the lln^s 1 have indicated. I hear that a man, unknown to anybody em
ployed !n Th~ w< rkp, wus seen around the- neighborhood, and i shall en
deavor t'> lind out who he wmk.
A. R. VEALE, Sheriff of Contra Costa County.

Once again the little town of Pinole
Is wrapped in sorrow and its flags are
at half-mast. In four houses death
has again entered suddenly, and for
the first time in the history of its pow
der explosions there Is more than a
suspicion that the catastrophe of yes
terday morning was the work of a
murderous miscreant.
During the eighteen sears since the
powder works were first bttilt, Pinole
has become accustomed to periodical
explosions. The mothers aud children
live In an atmosphere of i.orrible an
ticipation, and as soon as the direful
boom â the sound of which is so well
known â comes over the hill, there Is
but one thought In every mind.
Yesterday morning, not quite a cou
ple of hours after midnight, the awful
H'.UTul was heard for the first time in
three years. Instantly the village was
awake and at the windows and in the
go to the scene, not knowing what they
might find, but believing that their ser
vices would be needed.
As none of them returned, and as
no deaths were reported, it was pre
sumed that the explosion was trivial
and that no one was hurt. With the
coming of daylight, the town was reas
sured and the anxious Wives and
mothers were congratulating them
selves that aJI was well, when a sec
ond and far more terrible concussion
was heard and, instantly Pinole knew
that its worst expectations were real
ized. A few minutes later the truth
was known and when the large flag
that always flies in the center of the
town was only raised half-way, the
universal query was â "Who?"
Shortly before two o'clock yesterday
morning a slight detonation aroused
the sleepers at the Hercules Powder
Works and the officials at once started
out to investigate. Before many seconds
passed there was a loud explosion, that
was heard all over the town a mile
away. The practiced ears of the em
ployes who stay at the works, familiar
with such sound, at once traced its
source to the nitro-glycerine house,
which stands by itself on the extreme
northern edge of the company's prop
erty.
Assistant Superintendent Bermlng
ham mustered a gang of men and at
once started to clear away the wreck
age. Although it was very dark it was
considered inadvisable to wait for day
light, as there is always danger of the
debris taking fire and largely Increas
ing the danger to the surrounding
buildings. The men on the ground were
re-enforced by many who came in from
the town, and probably not less than
seventy-five were working under Su
perintendent Bermingham removing
the wreck an hour after the explosion
occurred. An inspection showed that
the damage had been confined to the
corner of the building occupied by what
is known as the blow-tank. This tank
is a massive iron affair and was blown
to pieces by the force of the explosive
that was within it. The brick founda
tion was also blown out and the under
pinning of the building badly under
mined. Beyond the wrecking of the
blow-tank no groat amount of damage
was done and no one was injured, as
the building was not near any of the
bunk houses.
Just at daybreak a workman named
Dan Frayer discovered a piece of
burned fuse, about four feet long. It
had but recently been burned out and
was in such a position as to suggest
that it had been placed under the blow
tank. Frayer at once called Mr. Berm
ingham to inspect it. and by his re
sponding promptly to the call the as
sistant superintendent saved his life,
for a moment later there was a severe
explosion on the main floor of the nitro
house, and Bermingham and two or
three others who were insDectiiiff the
THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, THTJESDAT, JULY 28, 1898.
The Wrecked Nitro Glycerine House in Which
Both Explosions Took Place.
fire were knocked down by flying de
bris.
The explosion that proved fatal and
that completed the wrecking of the
nitro house occurred about 5 o'clock.
Its force can only be estimated by the
completeness with which it wrecked
everything- in the vicinity aud the fear
ful havoc and death it dealt out to the
workmen who were nearest to It.
Before thr> smoke and dust had clear
ed away cries of wounded men were
heard from the many places where they
were pinned under the wreck. As the
survivors were returning to the scene,
having naturally run off to a s:ife dis
tance, they found a mass of d<wd hu
manity that was afterward recognized
a.-= that of Patrick Murphy. A little fur
ther, another body, horribly mutilated,
waa found, and whtle they were being
lifted from the mass of timber, iron
and shÂ«et lead, two other victims were
uneurthed. The four bodies were car
ried up the side of the hill and later
were taken into town by the Coroner,
where an inquest will be held on their
remains next Saturday.
Although tho actual damage to prop
erty is remarkably small, the list of in
jured is larger than in former explo
sions when fifty times the damage done
yesterday was experienced. As such, a
large number of men were at work
when the second explosion occmred, it
The Blow Tank Under Which it Is Supposed the
Fiend Placed the Fuse That Caused the
First Explosion.
Is remarkable that more were not
killed.
The effects of the explosions were
remarkable in many ways and bore
most striking testimony to the erratic
nature of nitro-glycerine â when ex
ploded. Although a strong iron tank
was twisted in all directions, glass
tubes and globes a few feet away were
not even cracked or disturbed. Al
though doors and stairs were sent a
hundred feet, many panes of glass were
left Intact, Some men were nearer to
the seat of the explosion than others
who were killed.
Frank Battis, proprietor of the Pinole
Times, was a witness of the explosion.
"While I was looking that way there
whs a loud report und a cloud of smoke
shot straight up In the air a distance
of several hundred feet," ho said. "I
saw all sorts of things flying around,
and without waiting for anything fur
ther ran off up the track, as I knew
some of the boys must have been
killed. It came to me like a flnsh when
I heard that a piece of burned fuse had
been found that a strange-looking In
dividual I had seen the evening before
might have been the mun responsible
for the first explosion. I saw him
about 8 o'clock. Instead of taking the
road or the railroad track up toward
the works from Pinole, he sneaked off
across the hills and could easily have
gotten into the powder company's in
closed property without being seen."
The officials of the works, together
with Sheriff Vrale, held a consultation
regarding the origin of the fuse found
near the blow tank.
"It was this fuse," said Assistant
Superintendent Berminghum, "which
destroyed the works. It was found
J this morning just at the east end of
| the mixing house by one of our men
and was given to me. You see that
| the fuse has been burned. But who
used It I don't know. Why he used It I
don't know. You can see what injury
it has done.
"I heard the explosion a little after
half-past 1 o'clock this morning. It
woke me up, and when I was preparing
to come here one of the men told me
what had happened. I came here and
a number of men followed. We went to
work to straighten out the wreck and
were at the job when the second ex
plosion took place. It demolished the
washing house, overturned some of the
washing tubes and shattered the wood
en and leaden tanks. At the time I
was on the hill just ' Immediately east
of the mixing house.
"I was hit on the bark with some
thing. I don't know what it was. It
hurt me a great deal and I feel the
effects of It. If I had been nearer the
washing house I would certainly have
fared worse. I was working where I
I was at the time, otherwise I would
j have been among the men who were i
; injured. I did not have any idea that
the washing house was in danger be
cause there was nothing in it that
could cause -an explosion. Mr. Quin
an's theory as to the cause of the accl
; dent is that in the explosion of the
tank some of the add was pro
jected Into the salt in the tanks in the
washing house.
"Thin is very plausible. The effect of
the explosion was terrific. I saw sev
eral men hurled through the air at a
tremendous rate. And yet, of two
men who were working side by side,
one was killed, and the other, though
blown a distance, escaped injury."
The man nearest to the scene of the
la.st explosion and who escaped injury
in an almost miraculous manner was
Chemist Smith, who has been con
nected with the works for the past
three years. Detailing his experiences
he said:
"I was in my room in bed at the
time. The explosion woke me up. 1
sleep in the laboratory about 2000 feet
away. I rose hastily and dressed my
self, first taking the precaution to open
all the windows. That is a precaution
which we always take under those cir
cumstances. I looked at the clock and
found it was twenty-five minutes to 2
o'clock. I took my lamp and went to
the nitro glycerine works and found the
top part gone. We started to clear
away the timbera. The men continued

to work until the second explosion took
place, and then they set to work to find
the bodies and aid the wounded.
"I was In a part of the structure
about twenty-five feet from the mass
of twisted Umber. That is the effect
of this kind of explosions. It twiata
timbers and breaks them off Just as
if they were given a heavy blow with
a mighty hammer.
"How I escaped I do not know, but
the force of the explosion RÂ«ms to
have gone in the direction opposite to
the place in which I was at the time.
The bodies of the poor men were
found on the hill side, all In nearly the
same relative position. They were hor
ribly mangled."
Patrick Murphy, one of the victims,
went to the scene of the explosion ear
ly this morning much against the
pleadings of his wife and family, who
had a misgiving that he would not re
turn alive.
The injured who were taken to St.
Mary's Hospital were all doing well
last night, except R. Bredhoff, who is
not considered out of danger.
The four injured men taken to
Fabiola Hospital on the special train
from the explosion were John B. Rice.
John Brunton, L. P. Evans and Edward
Healy. L. P. Evans, the foreman of the
acid department, sustained a bad frac
ture of his right arm and is badly
bruised and swollen. John Berming
ham Rice, who suffered a fractured leg,
graduated from Stanford with the
class of '79 and has quite a record as a
football player. These two are in the
most critical condition. All depends
upon the condition of their blood, and it
will be a week before the danger of
blood poisoning is entirely passed. They
are terribly sore to-night. John Brun
ton has four ribs badly shattered, but
is in a good way to recover. Edward
Healy has a badly crushed ankle, but
is the least injured of any. His
wounds were dressed, and time only
will tell whether he will ever be able to
use his foot again.

Olympia Bepublicans.
The Olympia Republican Club held a
largely attended meeting last Tuesday
evening at Its headquarters, California
Hall. 620 Bush street. J. D. Hart pre
sided. Judge J. Q. Severance was the
first speaker, and his remarks were en
thusiastically applauded. He was fol
lowed by G. W. Adams, M. R. Roberts
Jr., Dr. A. D. McLean, A. P. Wagner, A.
J. P. Regaudiat, Charles IÂ». Benton and
George R. Pidgeon. Benjamin Coonley,
an adept on the xylophone, rendered a
number of patriotic airs on that instru
ment. Mr. Brackett and W. C. R. Smith
followed Mr. Coonley with recitations,
and both were generously applauded.
Captain Howe, a veteran of the Civil
War,- made an effective speech.
